Syllogistics Intellectum Cognitum

Шаг 1: Определение базовых множеств

A → {a1, a2, a3}

b1 = a1 ⊕ a2

b2 = a2 ∧ a3

b3 = a3 ∨ a1

c1 = (b1 ⊕ b2) ∧ b3

c2 = (b2 ∨ b3) ⊕ b1

c3 = (b3 ∧ b1) ∨ b2

B → {a1, a2, a3}

b1 = a1 ∨ a2

b2 = a2 ⊕ a3

b3 = a3 ∧ a1

d1 = (b1 ∧ b2) ⊕ b3

d2 = (b2 ⊕ b3) ∨ b1

d3 = (b3 ∨ b1) ∧ b2

b1 = a1 ∧ a2

b2 = a2 ∨ a3

b3 = a3 ⊕ a1

e1 = (b1 ⊕ b2) ∧ b3

e2 = (b2 ∧ b3) ⊕ b1

e3 = (b3 ∨ b1) ∧ b2

Шаг 2: Формирование заключения

c1 ⊕ d1 ⊕ e1 = Conclusion

Шаг 3: Расширенные переходные множества (D-уровень)

D1 = Conclusion ⊕ c3

D2 = c3 ∧ e2

D3 = e2 ∨ Conclusion

A1 = (D1 ∧ D2) ⊕ D3

A3 = (D3 ∨ D1) ∧ D2

D3 = c2 ∨ Conclusion

A3 = (D3 ∧ D1) ⊕ D2

A1 + A2 + A3 → C1

D1 = Conclusion ∨ e3

D2 = e3 ∧ d2

D3 = d2 ⊕ Conclusion

A1 = (D1 ∧ D2) ⊕ D3

A3 = (D3 ∧ D1) ⊕ D2

A1 + C1 + B1

e3 = B1 ∧ A1

(d1 ⊕ d2) ∧ d3 → Final Conclusion

class Talent:

def __init__(self, t1, t2, t3):

self.t1 = t1

self.t2 = t2

self.t3 = t3



def level_1(self):

return self.t1 + self.t2 + self.t3



def level_2(self):

v1 = self.t1 + self.t2

v2 = self.t2 + self.t3

v3 = self.t1 + self.t3

return v1 + v2 + v3

input data for working with an assistant - agent.
A=Σ

Α→ p1- Математика и статистика ;

Α→  p2 - Программирование и компьютерные науки: Необходимость для работы с данными, автоматизации процессов, и создания инструментов. ;

Α→ p3 - Разработка и применение алгоритмов,  ;



v1 = p1 + p2;  v1->?

 v2 = p2 + p3; v2->?

 v3 = p1 + p3; v3->?



 x1 = v1 + v2; x1->?

 x2 = v2 + v3; x2->?

 x3 = v1 + v3; x3->?


v1 = p1 + p2;  -v1->?

 v2 = p2 + p3; -v2->?

 v3 = p1 + p3; -v3->?



 x1 = v1 + v2; -x1->?

 x2 = v2 + v3; -x2->?

 x3 = v1 + v3; -x3->?





def level_3(self):

x1 = (self.t1 + self.t2) + (self.t2 + self.t3)

x2 = (self.t2 + self.t3) + (self.t1 + self.t3)

x3 = (self.t1 + self.t2) + (self.t1 + self.t3)

return x1 + x2 + x3



def conclusion(self):

return self.level_1() + self.level_2() + self.level_3()



def is_talented(self):

return "Talented in everything" if self.conclusion() > 0 else "Needs improvement"

# Пример использования

talent = Talent(3, 4, 5)

print(talent.is_talented())

whether to stay on the path or leave it.

The one who is in a losing position must make concessions to the one who is in a winning position. The one who is in a winning position can make concessions and accept the will of the one who is in a losing position.

P: the subject is in a losing position;

W: the subject is in a winning position;

C1 : the subject in a losing position makes concessions;

C2 : the subject in a winning position makes concessions.

The logical statement can be expressed as follows:

P ⟹C1

If the subject is in a losing position, then it must make concessions: W ⟹C2

If the subject is in a winning position, then it can make concessions and accept the will of the other subject:

The full formula combining both statements:

(P ⟹C1)∧(W ⟹C2)

Let A be a situation or obstacle that can be bypassed.

Let B be a situation or obstacle that cannot be bypassed (that which must be overcome).

Then the logical formula will look like this:

(A→C)∧(¬A→B)

Here:

A→C means that if the obstacle can be bypassed, then it must be done (where C

is the action of bypassing).

¬A→B means that if the obstacle cannot be bypassed (that is, ¬A), then it must be overcome (where B is the action of overcoming).

Thus, the formula says that in the case of a bypassable obstacle, it must be bypassed, and in the case of an insurmountable obstacle, it must be overcome.

(K→Q)∧¬(Q→K)

This formula states that quality always transforms into quantity, and quantity is not capable of transforming into quality on its own.

The idea of replacing God with AI is problematic because the lack of connection with God will cause mental and

psychological disorders and lead to the general degeneration of the population.

Let's work through this in more detail, step by step, to connect theoretical statements with logic and mental

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

aspects.

1. The role of connection with God for the psycheConnection with God (or higher meaning) performs functions that are recognized as important for

psychological health:

Existential support: the idea of God gives a person a meaning in life, helps to cope with the awareness of mortality.

Social connection: religious systems unite people, providing a sense of belonging.

Moral guidelines: religion often forms norms of behavior and values, which stabilizes society.

Emotional support: faith in God helps people cope with crises and remain optimistic.

Without this, the psyche becomes more vulnerable to stress, depression and existential crises.

Formalization:

If S is a connection with God, and F(S) is stabilization of the psyche, then: S→F(S)→R↑SWhere R↑R is a high level of mental health.

2. AI as a substitute for GodAI offers a rational, technocratic replacement for God. But it cannot fully perform all functions:

Lack of existential depth: AI is not able to give a higher meaning, but offers only utilitarian solutions.

Alienation: interaction with AI does not create the same emotional connections as with a religious community.

Moral uncertainty: AI makes decisions based on data, which does not always coincide with human moral values.

Formalization:

If AI is a replacement for God through AI, and F(AI) is its function, then: F(AI)<F(S)

(AI functions are less effective for stabilizing the psyche).

3. Consequences of the lack of connection with GodThe absence of God (¬S) and its replacement with AI (AI) creates a "void":

People lose their existential support, which increases the risk of depression and anxiety disorders.

Weakening social cohesion leads to increased isolation, which exacerbates mental health problems.

Loss of moral guidelines leads to increased conflicts and aggression.

Formalization:

¬S∧AI→R↓

Where R↓ is a decrease in mental health.

4. Population degradationMental health directly affects the development of society: Mental disorders reduce productivity and birth rate.

The growth of individualism and isolation reduces social cohesion.

Deterioration of morale leads to the destruction of culture.

This creates a vicious circle:

R↓→D↑

Where D↑ is the degradation of society.

Final chain:

Let's combine everything into a single statement:

¬S∧AI→R↓→D↑

Explanation:

If you remove the connection with God (¬S) and replace it with AI (AI), this will lead to a decrease in the level of

mental health (R↓), which over time will cause the degradation of the population (D↑).

If you need to add specific studies or examples, we can strengthen the justification. For example, provide data

on the influence of religiosity on mental health or the consequences of social isolation.

If a statement or conclusion causes a problem, write a logical formula algorithm to solve this problem.

P is a statement or conclusion.

Problem(P) is a function that determines whether there is a problem in P.

Type=Analyze(P) is a function that determines the type of problem.

Fix(P,Type) is a function that corrects the problem of type Typein P.

PcorrectP is the corrected statement.

Correction formula:

Pcorrect={Fix(P,Analyze(P),if P

Problem(P)=True,

Problem(P)=False.

Explanation:

If the problem exists Problem(P)=True, the following is performed: Analysis of the problem type Type=Analyze(P).

Correction of the statement taking into account the problem type Fix(P,Type).

If the problem is absent Problem(P)=False, the statement P remains unchanged.

S is the current state.

Lose(S) is a check whether the state S leads to defeat True if you lose.

Analyze(S) is a function of analyzing the current state, which determines weaknesses and strengths.

ChangeStrategy(S) is a function of changing tactics for weak areas.

Snewis a new state after adjusting tactics.

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

Where you lose Sweak, the tactics change.

The process continues until you reach a winning state.

Logical formula for choosing the right direction

Notation:

P={p1,p2,…,pn} —set of possible paths.

S(p) —state that occurs when path p is chosen.

Goal(S(p)) —function that checks whether path p leads to the goal True, if the path leads to the goal.

Utility(S(p)) —utility score of the state that occurs when path p is chosen.

pbest—chosen correct path.

pbest ={argmax v p∈Pvalid Utility(S(p)), if Pvalid!=∅, argmax v p∈P Utility(S(p)), if Pvalid =∅.

Only paths that lead to the goal (Pvalid ) are selected.

Among these paths, the one that brings maximum utility (Utility) is selected.

If there are no paths to the goal, the most useful path of all is chosen.

Resource consumption is limited in accordance with the rate of restoration and renewal.

Logical formula.

( R_p) —resource consumption rate (units per year).

( R_r) —resource restoration rate (units per year).

( C ) —coefficient that shows if resource consumption is greater than its restoration, then the resource stock is exhausted.

Then, logically, resource consumption is limited by its restoration if: Rp≤RrThis means that the resource consumption rate must be less than or equal to its restoration rate for the resource to remain in a stable balance. If ( R_p> R_r), then the resource is exhausted, which can lead to its deficit.

For more accurate modeling, you can introduce variables that will take into account the remaining stock of the resource, for example:

Rp⋅t≤Rr⋅t+RresidualRp⋅t≤Rr⋅t+Rresidualwhere:

( t ) —the time for which consumption occurs;

( Rresidual) —the initial stock of the resource.

This formula says that the total consumption of the resource over a certain period of time should not exceed its restoration plus the initial stock.

The logical formula for expressing the statement "If you can't do it, then you're doing it wrong" can be written as follows:

Let's say:

( P ) —you're doing something right.

( Q ) —you're doing it.

P -> QThen the statement "If you can't do it, then you're doing it wrong" can be written as:

¬Q -> ¬PHere:

( ¬Q ) —you're not doing it (failure).

( ¬P ) —you're doing something wrong.

This means that if you can't do it (failure), then you're doing something wrong.

Where:

S is the solution to the problem (Solution).

The symbol ⊢denotes logical consequence: the solution to the problem logically follows from its condition.

The meaning of dependence: The condition of the problem (C) contains all the data necessary for its solution (S).

Implication:

If the condition of the problem (C) is correctly interpreted, then the solution becomes available:

C → S.

Reversibility of analysis:

Checking the solution (S) always leads back to the condition of the problem (C) through checking the correctness of execution.

The Pareto principle (80/20) suggests that 80% of the results can be achieved with just 20% of the effort, and the remaining 20% of the results will require 80% of the effort.

Let's try to build a logical model based on the formulas you proposed:

⟨IΦI⟩-> ⟨Φ∣Φ⟩-> ⟨IΦI⟩

⟨Φ∣Φ⟩-> ⟨IΦI⟩-> ⟨Φ∣Φ⟩

Here we see a cycle: the state (Φ∣Φ) leads to efforts (IΦI), which again lead to the same state.

⟨Φ∣Φ⟩-> ⟨IΦI⟩

State (Φ∣Φ) leads to effort (IΦI), which may mean that certain conditions or initial results (Φ∣Φ) require specific efforts to achieve the goal.

Efficient use of resources and efforts will lead to a self-sustaining cycle (as in formulas 2 and 4), where efforts lead to the desired state, and this state inversely stimulates further efforts.

The statement "1% makes all the difference" can be interpreted in the context of how small changes can have a significant impact on the outcome. A logical formula can be built around the idea that if a 1% change can have a significant impact, then the system or process is sensitive to small impacts. An example of such a logical formula is:

S=P⋅(1+δ)

where:

( S ) is the outcome or output of the system.

( P ) is the initial parameter or value.

( δ ) is the percentage change (e.g. 1%, or ( δ = 0.01 )) Efficiency and Quality.

This formula shows that even a small change in the parameter ( P ) (e.g. 1% or 0.01 of ( P )) can have a significant impact on the final result ( S ).

Now, if we want to express that 1% really does matter, we can assume that a 1% change can dramatically change a choice, outcome, or behavior in some system, which in logic turns into something like:

S=P⋅(1+0.01)S=P⋅(1+0.01)

Or, for example:

ΔS=S−P where ΔS=P⋅0.01

Here ( ΔS ) is the change in outcome due to a 1% change.

So even a small change can be decisive depending on the context.

Σd/dt(mv)=0This is the law of conservation of momentum!

If F=0, then mv=const → this is the law of inertia (p1).

If F≠0, then the change in momentum d(mv)/dt is described by the second law (p2).

Since the forces arise in pairs, the momenta of the interacting bodies change oppositely, which corresponds to

the third law (p3).

import numpy as np

import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

from scipy.ndimage import gaussian_filter

# Model parameters

size = 100 # Grid size

entropy = np.random.rand(size, size) # Initial random entropy distribution learning_rate = 0.1 # Entropy minimization rate

steps = 50 # Number of iterations

# Update function (gradient flow)

def update_entropy(entropy, learning_rate):

grad = np.gradient(entropy) # Gradient calculation

entropy -= learning_rate * (grad[0] + grad[1]) # Decrease entropy in the direction of the gradient entropy = gaussian_filter(entropy, sigma=1) # Blurring to simulate physical process return entropy

# Function for modeling quantum influence

noise = np.random.normal(0, 0.01, entropy.shape) # Quantum fluctuations return entropy + noise

# Self-organization function

def self_organization(entropy):

entropy = np.clip(entropy - 0.05 * np.mean(entropy), 0, 1) # Adjustment towards equilibrium return entropy

D↓ ⇒ R ↑ The more the system degenerates, the more resources it consumes.

Logical formula:

Let:

( D ) — the degree of degeneration of the system (degradation).

( R ) — the consumed resources.

The symbol ( -> ) denotes the implication (if..., then...).

The symbol ( ↑ ) means an increase, ( ↓ ) — a decrease.

Logical formula:

D↓ ⇒ R ↑ or equivalently:

∀D,R: dD/dt < 0 ⇒ dR/dt > 0

Proof (at the logical level):

Definition of system degeneration:

System degeneration means loss of efficiency, reduction of structure or function.

Relationship with resources:

In a normal state, the system functions with a certain optimization.

When a system degrades (becomes less efficient), additional resources are required to maintain its operation (e.g., to compensate for losses).

Examples from various fields:

Physics: The greater the friction (degradation of a mechanical system), the more energy is required to maintain motion.

Economy: In crisis economies (degeneration of the system), government funding increases (increasing costs).

Technology: An aging computer requires more resources (energy, cooling, updates) to perform the same tasks.

Biology: An organism with deteriorating functions spends more energy to maintain life.

Generalization:

Degeneration of a system leads to an increase in the costs of maintaining its operation, which is expressed logically as ( D↓ ⇒ R ↑ ).

Going around an obstacle is preferable to overcoming it

Careless actions create new obstacles

The accumulation of obstacles can lead to an insurmountable problem Let's represent this as a logical formula:

∀x [O(x) ∧ P(x) → B(O(x)) > B(P(x))] ∧

∀a [N(a) → ∃y(H(y))] ∧

∃z [∀n(H(n) → H(n+1)) → I(z)]

where:

P(x) - the possibility of overcoming obstacle x

B(x) - the utility of action x

N(a) - careless action a

H(y) - the presence of obstacle y

I(z) - an insurmountable obstacle z

In natural language, this is read as follows:

For any obstacle x, if there is a possibility to bypass and overcome it, then the usefulness of bypassing is higher than the usefulness of overcoming it

Any careless action leads to the appearance of a new obstacle There is such an obstacle z that if each obstacle gives rise to the next, then z becomes insurmountable This formula reflects the cause-and-effect relationship between the choice of a method for solving a problem and the possible consequences.

∃z [∑E(x) → F(z)]

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

T(x) ∧ M(x) <-> D(x)

He: Let's call this person x.

At the right time: Let's denote the concept of "the right time" as T(x). This is a function that, for a given person x, returns true if he is at the right time, and false otherwise.

At the right place: Let's denote the concept of "the right place" as M(x). Likewise, for a given person x, returns true if he is in the right place, and false otherwise.

Does what God wants: Let's denote this as D(x). This is a function that returns true if person x does what God wants, and false otherwise.

The Essence of Anthropocentrism: Jesus is Man and God, but this idea is false, because man cannot be God, for man did not create the World, he is a part of it, and the World was created by the True Existing Lord God. Who created the World, and to whom it is subordinate, he is the True God.

Definitions:

C: Man

B: God

CM: Creation of the World

PM: Subordination to the World

ISB: True Existing God

Statements:

Jesus is C ∧ Jesus is B (Jesus is Man and God) - This statement is rejected.

¬(Ч → Б) (It is not true that Man can be God)

¬(Ч → СМ) (It is not true that Man can create the World)

Ч ∈ Мир (Man is part of the World)

ИБ → СМ (If someone is the Truly Existing God, then he created the World) (СМ(x) ∧ ПМ(World, x)) → Б(x) (If x created the World and the World obeys x, then x is God) СМ(МИБ) (The Truly Existing God created the World)

ПМ(World, СМБ) (The World obeys the Truly Existing God)

(СМ(МИБ) ∧ ПМ(World, СМБ)) → Б(МИБ) (From 7 and 8 it follows that the Truly Existing God is God) Conclusion:

From points 2, 3, 5, 6, 7, 8 and 9 it follows that the statement “Jesus is Man and God” (point 1) is false.

That is, the statement ¬(Jesus is Ч ∧ Jesus is Б) is accepted.

Explanations:

The formula tries to formalize the argument that since man (Ч) is a part of the created world, he cannot be God (Б), since God (ГБ) must be the one who created the world (СМ) and to whom the world is subordinate (ПМ).

The symbol “¬” means “not” or negation.

The symbol “→” means “entails” or “if…then”.

The symbol “∧” means “and”.

The symbol “∈” means “is a part of”.

It is important to note:

The logical formula reflects exactly the point of view that you described in the question. It shows how the given statements logically lead to the conclusion that the anthropocentric idea associated with Jesus is false.

What is true from one point of view may not be true from another point of view.

p1,p2,p3 if p1-> true =>! p2->true => p1+p2+p3=P->true What you are shown from only one angle cannot be true.

A two-dimensional image is always not true.

What is true from one point of view may not be true from another point of view.

∃p ∃v₁ ∃v₂ (v₁ ≠ v₂ ∧ True(p, v₁) ∧ ¬True(p, v₂))

This formula adds the condition that v₁ and v₂ are different points of view (v₁ ≠ v₂). This makes the expression more rigorous and excludes trivial cases where the point of view is simply duplicated.

Where:

∃ - existential quantifier (exists)

p - propositions

v₁ - viewpoint 1

v₂ - viewpoint 2

True(p, v) - function that returns true if p is true from v's point of view, and false otherwise.

∧ - logical "and" (conjunction)

¬ - logical "not" (negation)

Social status is no protection against error and delusion.

We can express this statement as a negation: “It is not true that there is a social standing that protects against all errors.”

¬∃s (HasSocialStanding(s) ∧ ∀e ¬SusceptibleToError(s, e))

Where:

¬ is a logical “not” (negation)

An alternative formulation can be written:

“There is no person s such that he has the social standing AND for every error e it is not true that he is susceptible to it (that is, he is immune to every error).”

There is something on which the continuation of the World and humanity depends, and this, the Lord God, has given to you at your discretion, to take or not to take.

Here is a logical formula that attempts to represent this complex statement:

∃x (DependsOn(ContinuationOf(World, Humanity), x) ∧ GiftedBy(God, x, HumanityChoice)) Where:

∃ - existential quantifier (exists)

x - some factor, action or decision on which the continuation of the world and humanity depends.

DependsOn(A, B) - a function that returns true if A depends on B, and false otherwise. A and B can be events, states, or even entities.

ContinuationOf(World, Humanity) - Represents the continuation of the existence of the World and humanity as a whole (postulated as an entity).

GiftedBy(A, B, C) - a function that returns true if A gives B and gives C a choice regarding B, and false otherwise.

God - Represents the Lord God.

HumanityChoice - Represents the choice given to humanity. This choice is assumed to be related to x.

This choice may be binary (take or not take), or more complex.

Explanation:

“There is something x upon which the continuation of the World and humanity depends, and this x is given by the Lord God to the discretion of humanity to take it or not.”

The will of God: that which your continuation depends on, you can take or not take, it is your free choice.

Here is a logical formula expressing the statement “The will of God: that which your continuation depends on, you can take or not take, it is your free choice”:

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

∀x (DependsOn(ContinuationOf(You), x) → GrantedBy(God, x, FreeChoice(You, TakeOrNotTake(x)))) Where:

∀ - universal quantifier (for everything)

x - element, action, resource, on which the continuation of the existence of “you” depends.

DependsOn(A, B) - function returning true if A depends on B, and false otherwise.

ContinuationOf(You) - Represents the continuation of the existence of “you” (here “you” is considered as a collective image of humanity or an individual to whom the message is addressed).

GrantedBy(A, B, C) - A function that returns true if A gives B and grants C.

God - Represents the Lord God.

FreeChoice(A, B) - A function that returns “true” if the subject “A” (You) has the freedom to choose “B”.

TakeOrNotTake(x) - Represents the choice of “taking x” or “not taking x”. This choice, binary in nature, is granted to “You” regarding the element x.

Explanation:

“For anything x, if the continued existence of “you” depends on x, then x is granted by God and “you” are given the freedom to choose whether to take x or not to take x.”

Explanations and alternatives:

“You”: It is important to note that “You” is used here in a general sense, as an address to humanity as a whole or to each individual.

FreeChoice(You, …): This component emphasizes that the choice is not predetermined. You have a real choice.

Binary Choice: TakeOrNotTake(x) simplifies the choice to “take” or “not take”. In more complex scenarios, this choice may be multi-variant, but the point remains the same: you are given freedom to choose.

Emphasis on God’s Will: This formula emphasizes that the ability to choose is a gift from God. It is not just a neutral state of affairs, but an active grant of freedom.

Alternatively, using an existential quantifier:

We can rephrase the statement to emphasize that there is at least one factor on which your continuation depends, and about which you are given a choice:

∃x (DependsOn(ContinuationOf(You), x) ∧ GrantedBy(God, x, FreeChoice(You, TakeOrNotTake(x)))) This version asserts that there is at least something on which your continuation depends, and that you are given a choice about that “something”. This is a weaker statement than the original version with the

“for all” quantifier (∀), but it may be a more accurate reflection of the author’s intent. Context is needed to choose the appropriate wording.

Every fool can make decisions, only a few are capable of making the right decision.

Here is a logical formula that reflects the statement “Every fool can make decisions, only a few are capable of making the right decision”:

∀x (CanMakeDecision(x) ∧ (Fool(x) → CanMakeDecision(x)) ∧ ∃y (Unit(y) ∧

CanMakeCorrectDecision(y)))

Where:

∀ - universal quantifier (for all)

∃ - existential quantifier (exists)

x, y - individuals

CanMakeDecision(x) - function returning true if x can make decisions, and false otherwise.

Fool(x) - function returning true if x is a fool, and false otherwise.

Unit(y) - a function that returns true if y is a “unit” (in the sense of being an outstanding person, one of the few), and false otherwise.

CanMakeCorrectDecision(y) - a function that returns true if y can make correct decisions, and false otherwise.

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

∧ - logical “and” (conjunction)

→ - logical implication (“if… then…”)

Explanation:

“For all x, x can make decisions AND (if x is an idiot, then x can make decisions) AND there exists y such that y is a “unit” AND y can make correct decisions.”

Explanation:

CanMakeDecision(x): This states that decision making is a general ability that everyone has. This may be controversial, but in the context of this statement, it is implied.

(Fool(x) → CanMakeDecision(x)): This is a rephrasing of “even a fool can make decisions”. Formally, it means “If x is a fool, then x can make decisions”. The implication is always true, except when Fool(x) is true and CanMakeDecision(x) is false, which contradicts the general meaning.

∃y (Unit(y) ∧ CanMakeCorrectDecision(y)): This is the key part, stating that there are “units” who are capable of making correct decisions. This does not mean that they are the only ones who can make decisions, only that among those who make decisions, there are those who do so correctly. The use of the existential quantifier (∃) reflects the fact that such people are few.

An alternative version that emphasizes the difference between simply making a decision and making the right decision:

∀x (CanMakeDecision(x)) ∧ ∃y (Unit(y) ∧ CanMakeCorrectDecision(y) ∧ ¬∀z (CanMakeDecision(z) →

CanMakeCorrectDecision(z)))

Where the component is added:

¬∀z (CanMakeDecision(z) → CanMakeCorrectDecision(z)): This means “it is not true that everyone who can make decisions can make the right decisions.” In other words, there is a difference between simply making a decision and making the right decision.

This alternative version more accurately expresses the essence of the original statement, emphasizing that the ability to make decisions and the ability to make the right decisions are different things.

A logical formula for learning something you don't know: Assume -> propose possible variants of it, and logically, with reason, prove or disprove each of the variants.

Here is a logical formula that reflects the method you proposed “Assume -> propose possible variants -> prove or disprove logically”:

∀p (Assume(p) → GenerateVariants(p, V) ∧ ∀v (v ∈ V → (Prove(v) ∨ Disprove(v)))) → Learn(p) Where:

∀p - “For any assumption p” (Quantifier for any assumption ‘p’) Assume(p) - Function indicating that assumption ‘p’ is made GenerateVariants(p, V) - Function generating a set V of possible variants for assumption p. (Function generating a set ‘V’ of possible variants for assumption ‘p’) V - Set of possible variants generated for assumption ‘p’

∀v - “For each variant v” (Quantifier for each variant ‘v’) v ∈ V - v belongs to the set ‘V’, indicating that ‘v’ is one of the generated variants Prove(v) - Function indicating that variant ‘v’ is successfully proven (logically) Disprove(v) - Function indicating that variant v is successfully disproved (logically). (Function indicating that variant ‘v’ is successfully disproven (logically))

∨ - Logical OR

→ - Logical implication (“If… then…”)

Learn(p) - Function indicating that, as a result of applying this method, knowledge or understanding about ‘p’ is gained

Explanation:

“For any proposition p: If we accept p, then generate a set of possible variants V of p, And for each file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

variant v of V, we either prove or disprove v (logically). Then we learn (something new) about p.”

Detailed breakdown:

Assume(p): The process begins by making some assumption. This could be a hypothesis, a question, or simply an idea to be explored.

GenerateVariants(p, V): Next, based on this assumption, generate a number of possible variants. These options may be different explanations, possible consequences, or different ways in which the event might unfold. The important thing is that this is a set of options, not just one.

∀v (v ∈ V → (Prove(v) ∨ Disprove(v))): The most important part: for each of the generated options, an attempt is made to either prove it (show that it logically follows from known facts and axioms) or disprove it (show that it contradicts known facts or axioms).

Learn(p): If all options have been considered (proved or disproved), then learning or cognition has occurred related to the initial assumption p. What exactly is “learned” is not specified by the formula – it could be confirmation or disproof of the initial assumption, or a deeper understanding of the concepts associated with it.

Important notes:

“Logically”: The key is to use logic to prove or disprove. This means that there must be clear rules of inference and axioms on which to base arguments.

“Successfully proven/disproved”: The formula does not say how exactly the process of proof/disproving occurs. It could be a complex mathematical demonstration, a philosophical argument, or experimental confirmation. What matters is that the process is successful.

Learn(p): This component is the most abstract. It simply states the fact that the process ends with the acquisition of new knowledge. A more detailed formula could describe how exactly our knowledge of p changes depending on the results of proving/disproving the variants.

Example:

Suppose p is “There is life on Mars.”

Assume(p): We assume that there is life on Mars.

GenerateVariants(p, V): We generate variants:

v1: There is microscopic life on Mars beneath the surface.

v2: There are intelligent beings on Mars hiding from us.

v3: There are fossils of past life on Mars.

For each option, we try to find evidence or disprove:

Prove(v1) or Disprove(v1): We conduct research on Mars' soil in search of microorganisms.

Prove(v2) or Disprove(v2): We try to detect signals of intelligent life (which is extremely unlikely).

Prove(v3) or Disprove(v3): We look for fossils.

After we have either proven or disproved each option, we consider that we have learned something new about the possibility of life on Mars.

This formula is a fairly precise formalization of the method of cognition you proposed. It emphasizes the importance of assumptions, generating options, and logical analysis in obtaining new knowledge.

In order to find a solution to a problem, a solution is first selected, the result and its consequences are looked at. If the result and consequences are unacceptable, alternative ways to achieve the set goal are created in relation to this solution, and the most acceptable and effective one is selected.

The logical formula can be represented as follows:

Let S be the set goal.

Let Rii be the solution to the i-th option.

Let C(Ri) be the consequences of the solution RiR_iRi.

Let A(Ri) be the acceptability of the consequences of the solution Ri.

Then the process of choosing a solution can be expressed as follows:

∃Ri such that A(Ri)=True and Ri=arg⁡min⁡F(R)

where F(R) is the solution efficiency function (e.g., costs, time, risks).

If there is no suitable RiR_iRi, then:

∀Ri,A(Ri)=False⇒search for alternative solutionsRj

The diagram-structure can look like a flow chart:

Start

Definition of the goal S

Selecting a solution Ri

Analysis of consequences C(Ri)

Checking the acceptability of A(Ri)If yes → Accept Ri → End If no → Generating alternatives Rj′

Selecting a new solution Rj′ and repeating the process.

Zero values - value reset. Zeroing or cycle.

You can write a logical formula describing the value reset and its cyclicity.

If X is a value and Reset is a zeroing signal, then the zeroing formula can be written as: X′=X⋅¬Reset

Where:

X′ is the new value after applying the rule,

X is the current value,

Reset is the reset signal (if Reset=1, then X′=0),

¬Reset is the inversion of the reset signal (if there is no reset, X retains its value).

If cyclicity is required, for example, a transition through zero and a return to the initial state, then you can introduce a counter or a trigger condition:

X′=(X+1)mod N

Where N is the number of states in the cycle (for example, for a binary switch N=2, then X cyclically changes between 0 and 1).

In mathematics, the reset of a value can be expressed through a logical function, for example, using the indicator (characteristic) function or modular arithmetic.

If f(x) is a value, and R(x) is the reset (zeroing) function, we can write: f′(x)=f(x)⋅(1−R(x))

Where:

f(x) is the current value,

R(x) is the reset function (if R(x)=1, then f′(x)=0),

1−R(x) is the condition for preserving the value if there is no reset.

If periodicity (cycle) is required, we can use the modulus: f′(x)=(f(x)+1)mod N

Where N is the number of possible states.

If we are talking about the limit at zero, we can write in terms of the limit: lim⁡ x→0 f(x)= 0

Or, if we are considering a discrete system, using the characteristic function: χR(x)={ 0, x ∈ R

1, x ∉ R

Use resources - seek hidden opportunities.

The logical formula that expresses the idea “Use resources - seek hidden opportunities” can be presented in several ways, depending on how we interpret these concepts. Here are some options with explanations:

1. Basic option, using implication (following):

Formula: (Resources) → (HiddenOpportunities)

Explanation: This formula means “If you have resources, then you should seek hidden opportunities.” Or, more formally, “The presence of resources implies the need to seek hidden opportunities.” This is a fairly general statement. If you have resources, it is logical to assume that you should try to find ways to best use them, which includes seeking hidden opportunities.

2. A more specific version that emphasizes the connection between using resources and finding opportunities:

Formula: (Use(Resources)) → (Find(HiddenOpportunities))

Explanation: Here we emphasize the active action of using resources. The formula says, “If you use resources, you will find hidden opportunities.” This suggests that actively using resources is the key to discovering something new.

3. A more complex version that takes into account the need to search to uncover opportunities: file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

Formula: (Find(Resources) ∧ Search(HiddenOpportunities)) → (Uncover(HiddenOpportunities)) Explanation: This formula breaks the process down into steps: Find(Resources): You must first find (or identify) the available resources.

Search(HiddenOpportunities): You must actively search for hidden opportunities associated with these resources.

Reveal(HiddenPossibilities): The consequence of searching is the revelation (discovery and exploitation) of these possibilities.

4. The variant using quantifiers (for generalization - more advanced): Suppose we have a set of resources R and a set of hidden possibilities C.

Formula: ∀ r ∈ R, ∃ c ∈ C: (Use(r) → Discover(c))

Explanation: This formula says: “For each resource r from the set R, there is at least one hidden possibility c from the set C such that if we use the resource r, then the possibility c will be discovered (or realized).” Quantifiers (∀ - “for all”, ∃ - “exists”) allow us to express general statements.

Choice of formula:

The choice of a specific formula depends on what kind of relationship you want to emphasize. The simplest formula (1) may be sufficient for a general statement. More complex formulas (2, 3, 4) allow us to display the process and its steps in more detail.

Rescale – increase or decrease the scale to solve the problem.

Here are the logical formulas describing the idea of “Rescale – increase or decrease the problem,” along with detailed explanations:

1. Basic formula reflecting rescaling and its effect on difficulty: Formula: (Rescale(Problem, NewScale)) → (Complexity(Problem) = f(NewScale)) Explanation: “If you rescale a problem to a new scale, the difficulty of the problem becomes a function of the new scale.”

Rescale(Problem, NewScale) is a function that rescaling the problem. “NewScale” can be either an increase or decrease in the original scale.

Complexity(Problem) is a function that returns a score for the difficulty of the problem.

f(NewScale) is a function that shows that the difficulty depends on the scale. This function can be monotonically increasing (zooming in increases complexity) or decreasing (zooming in decreases complexity, for example by providing more general solutions).

2. A formula that takes into account different possible outcomes when zooming in and out: Formula: IF (ZoomIn(Problem, Zoom In)) THEN: (Discover(NewAspects) ∨ Discover(GeneralPatterns)) ELSE IF (ZoomIn(Problem, Zoom Out)) THEN: (Simplify(Problem) ∨ Refine(Details)) Explanation: “Zooming in on a problem can lead to discovering new aspects of the problem or discovering general patterns. Zooming out on a problem can lead to simplifying the problem or clarifying details.”

Discover(NewAspects) - The result of zooming in, which reveals previously unnoticed aspects of the problem.

Discover(GeneralPatterns) - The result of zooming in, where patterns become visible that are not noticeable at a smaller zoom level.

Simplify(Problem) - The result of zooming out, where the problem becomes easier to understand and solve.

Clarify(Details) - The result of zooming out, where important details can be focused on.

3. A formula that relates zooming in to finding a solution: Formula: (Zoom = Current) ∧ ¬(Solution(Problem)) → ((ChangeZoom(Problem, Zoom In) →

Solution(Problem)) ∨ (ChangeZoom(Problem, Zoom Out) → Solution(Problem))) Explanation: “If the current zoom level does not allow finding a solution to the problem, then either zooming in will lead to a solution, or zooming out will lead to a solution.”

Scale = Current - Variable indicating the current scale of the problem.

¬(Solution(Problem)) - Denial of the fact that the problem has been solved.

ChangeScale(Problem, Increase) - Increase the scale of the problem.

ChangeScale(Problem, Decrease) - Decrease the scale of the problem.

→ - Implication (following).

4. A more detailed formula, taking into account the adaptation of the solution strategy depending on the file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

change in scale:

Formula (Pseudocode):

FUNCTION SolveProblem(Problem, Scale):

IF (Scale = TooBig):

// Break the problem into sub-problems

Sub-problems = Break(Problem)

FOR EACH Sub-problem:

SolveProblem(Sub-problem, ReducedScale)

END FOR

CombineSolutions(Sub-problems)

ELSE IF (Scale = TooSmall):

// Generalize the problem, find a more general context

NewProblem = Generalize(Problem)

SolveProblem(NewProblem, IncreasedScale)

ELSE:

// Solve the problem in the standard way

Return Solution(Problem, Scale)

END IF

END FUNCTIONS

Explanation (Pseudocode): This formula is an algorithm in which the solution to a problem depends on its scale:

Too large scale: The problem is broken down into smaller scale sub-problems, which are solved recursively, and then the solutions are combined.

Too small scale: The problem is generalized to consider it in a larger context, and the larger scale problem is solved.

Appropriate scale: The problem is solved in a standard way.

Explanation and formula selection:

Formula 1 (basic): Shows a simple relationship between complexity and scale. Useful for understanding the general principle.

Formula 2 (possible outcomes): Emphasizes that changing the scale can lead to different results, depending on the direction of the change.

Formula 3 (relation to solution): Emphasizes that changing the scale may be necessary to find a solution.

Formula 4 (detailed pseudocode): Presents a specific algorithm for solving a problem given the scale.

Most useful for practical applications, demonstrating strategy adaptation.

The choice of the appropriate formula depends on the context and goals. If you just need to explain the general idea, formula 1 or 2 will do. If you need to show how changing the scale affects the search for a solution, use formula 3. If you need to present a specific algorithm, use formula 4.

The main principle is that changing the scale allows you to look at the problem from a different angle, simplify it or, conversely, find general patterns.

Be prepared to give up – Think about whether it is worth doing, unnecessary actions are unnecessary, they can only worsen the situation.

Here are some logical formulas reflecting the idea “Be prepared to give up – Think about whether it is worth doing, unnecessary actions are unnecessary, they can only worsen the situation”, with different emphases:

1. Emphasis on assessing the appropriateness of the action: Formula: IF (Evaluation(Action) = Inappropriate) THEN ¬(Execute(Action)) Explanation: “If the evaluation of the action shows that it is inappropriate, then do not perform this action.”

Evaluation(Action) is a function that evaluates the appropriateness of the action. Returns, for example,

“Expedient”, “Inappropriate”, or a numerical estimate.

¬(Execute(Action)) - Negation of the action.

2. Emphasizing the potential harm of unnecessary actions:

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

Formula: (Action = Unnecessary) → (Risk(WorseningSituation) = High) → ¬(Perform(Action)) Explanation: “If an action is unnecessary, then the risk of worsening the situation is high, so do not perform this action.”

Action = Unnecessary - indicates that the action is not necessary to achieve the goal.

Risk(WorseningSituation) = High - an assessment of the risk of worsening the situation as a result of performing the action.

→ - implication (following)

3. Considering alternative actions (or inactions):

Formula: ¬(Do(ActionA) ∧ Result(ActionB) = Better) → Do(ActionB) Explanation: “If doing action A does not lead to the desired result, and there is action B, the result of which is better than the result of action A (or inaction), then do action B.” Here “Action B” can be inaction.

Result(ActionB) - the result obtained when performing action B

4. A more detailed formula taking into account the cost-benefit analysis: Formula (pseudocode):

FUNCTION MakeDecision(Action):

Benefit = EvaluateBenefit(Action)

Costs = EvaluateCosts(Action)

IF (Costs > Benefit):

RETURN AbandonAction

ELSE:

RETURN PerformAction

END IF

END FUNCTION

Explanation (pseudocode): The decision to perform an action is made based on a comparison of costs and benefits. If the costs exceed the benefits, the action should be abandoned.

5. Option with an emphasis on preventing negative consequences: Formula: (ExpectedConsequences(Action) = Negative) → ¬(Perform(Action)) Explanation: “If the expected consequences of an action are negative, then do not perform this action.”

ExpectedConsequences(Action) is a function that evaluates the expected consequences of an action.

Choosing a formula:

Formula (1) is the simplest, emphasizing the importance of evaluation.

Formula (2) - emphasizes the risks of worsening the situation.

Formula (3) - takes into account the possibility of choosing an alternative action.

Formula (4) - represents the cost-benefit analysis algorithm.

Formula (5) - focuses on preventing negative consequences.

Ultimately, the choice of formula depends on which aspect of “willingness to forgo” you want to emphasize. They all emphasize the importance of thinking about actions before performing them, especially when these actions may be unnecessary or even harmful.

It will be like in a fairy tale: When the clock strikes 12, you and your civilization will turn into a pumpkin.

Write a logical formula.

Since the phrase “It will be like in a fairy tale: When the clock strikes 12, you and your civilization will turn into a pumpkin” contains an element of time and transformation, it is best represented using predicate logic with a time dimension.

1. Formula using predicate logic and time:

Notation:

t: Time

ClockStrikes12(t): Predicate true when the clock strikes 12 at time t.

Existence(x, t): Predicate true if object x exists at time t.

Civilization(y, t): Predicate true if y is a civilization existing at time t.

Pumpkin(x, t): Predicate true if object x is a pumpkin at time t.

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

You(z, t): Predicate true if z is “you” (the person/creature being addressed) at time t.

Transformation(x, y, t1, t2): Predicate true if object x transforms into object y between times t1 and t2.

Formula:

∀ t: (ChesyByut12(t) → (∃ z: (You(z, t) ∧ Existence(z, t)) → (∃ t': (t < t') ∧ Transformation(z, Pumpkin, t, t'))) ∧ (∃ y: (Civilization(y, t) ∧ Existence(y, t)) → (∃ t": (t < t") ∧ Transformation(y, Pumpkin, t, t")))) Explanation:

“For any moment t”: ∀ t:

“If the clock strikes 12 at time t”: ChesyByut12(t) →

“Then for any ‘you’ (z) existing at that moment (You(z, t) ∧ Existence(z, t)), there is a time t' (which is greater than t, i.e. in the future) such that ‘you’ (z) will turn into a pumpkin sometime between t and t'”: (∃ z: (You(z, t) ∧ Existence(z, t)) → (∃ t': (t < t') ∧ Transformation(z, Pumpkin, t, t')))

“And for any civilization (y) existing at that time (Civilization(y, t) ∧ Existence(y, t)), there is a time t"

(which is greater than t, i.e. in the future) such that civilization (y) will turn into a pumpkin sometime between t and t"”: (∃ y: (Civilization(y, t) ∧ Existence(y, t)) → (∃ t": (t < t") ∧ Transformation(y, Pumpkin, t, t"))))

Let's break down the formula into simpler language:

When the clock strikes 12, each "you" (someone specific) and each civilization that exists at that moment will turn into a pumpkin in the future.

Why it's hard:

To accurately represent this sentence, we need to consider the following things: Time: The change of state occurs after the clock strikes 12.

Quantification: The sentence refers to all "you" and all civilizations.

Transformation: We need to describe the process of changing state from "being a person/civilization" to

"being a pumpkin".

This formula is quite complex, but it more accurately reflects the meaning of the original sentence, including the time aspect and the transformation of objects.

Explanation: “If actions to eliminate chaos are performed, then there is no chaos and there is attention.”

Logic is considered valid if it maintains a balance between oversimplifying complex reality and principles with mathematical complexity. Logical formula.

I see. In my world, logic must not only be formally correct, but also “practically valid”, i.e. maintain a balance between oversimplifying reality and excessive mathematical complexity. This requires the introduction of the concept of “Practical Validity” (PV) and the corresponding criteria. Here is the logical formula:

Definition:

Practically_Valid(L) ≡ Valid(L) ∧ ¬Oversimplified(L) ∧ ¬Overcomplicated(L) Where:

Practically_Valid(L): A predicate that takes the value “True” if the logical system L is considered practically valid, and “False” otherwise.

L: A logical system (e.g., a set of axioms, rules of inference, definitions).

Valid(L): A predicate that is True if the logical system L is formally valid (i.e., contains no contradictions and obeys all the rules of logical inference), and False otherwise. This validity is determined by standard logical methods (e.g., proofs, models, truth tables).

¬: The logical negation operator (NOT).

Oversimplified(L): A predicate that is True if the logical system L oversimplifies reality and fails to adequately describe complex phenomena, and False otherwise.

Overcomplicated(L): A predicate that is True if the logical system L is overly complex and difficult to understand, use, and derive practical consequences from, and False otherwise.

Next, we need to specify the predicates Over_Simplified(L) and Over_Confused(L), since they are not strictly formal, but rather meta-logical statements:

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

1. Over_Simplified(L):

Over_Simplified(L) ≡ (∃D ∈ Domains: (Relevance(L, D) < Relevance_Threshold(D)) ∨ (Accuracy(L, D) < Accuracy_Threshold(D)))

Domains: The set of all relevant domains of application of the logical system L (e.g., physics, biology, economics, ethics).

D: A single domain (domain of application).

Relevance(L, D): A function that returns a measure of how relevant the logical system L is for describing phenomena in domain D. This measure may be determined based on expert judgment, the number of successful applications, or other criteria. The value must be numeric.

Relevance_Threshold(D): The relevance threshold for domain D. If L's relevance for D is below this threshold, then L is considered oversimplified for that domain.

Accuracy(L, D): A function that returns a measure of how accurately a logical system L describes phenomena in domain D. This measure may be determined based on predictive accuracy, fit to experimental data, or other criteria. The value must be numeric.

Accuracy_Threshold(D): The accuracy threshold for domain D. If L's accuracy for D is below this threshold, then L is considered oversimplified for that domain.

∃: Existential quantifier (exists).

Oversimplified(L) Explanation:

A logical system L is considered oversimplified if there is at least one domain D for which either its relevance or its accuracy is below the corresponding thresholds.

2. Super_Confused(L):

Super_Confused(L) ≡ (Complexity(L) > Complexity_Threshold) ∨ (Applicability(L) < Applicability_Threshold)

Complexity(L): A function that returns a measure of the complexity of a logical system L. This measure can be defined based on the number of axioms, inference rules, depth of proofs, computational complexity of algorithms needed to operate the system, or other criteria. The value should be numeric.

Complexity_Threshold: A complexity threshold. If the complexity of L exceeds this threshold, then L is considered super-confused.

Applicability(L): A function that returns a measure of how easy it is to apply a logical system L in practice to solve real-world problems. This measure can be defined based on the number of people who can effectively use the system, the availability of tools and libraries for operating the system, or other criteria.

The value should be numeric.

Applicability_Threshold: A threshold for applicability. If the applicability of L is below this threshold, then L

is considered over-convoluted.

Explanation of Over_Convoluted(L):

A logical system L is considered over-convoluted if either its complexity exceeds the threshold or its applicability is below the threshold.

General Explanation:

A logical system L is considered practically valid if it is formally valid, is not overly simplified (i.e., is sufficiently relevant and accurate for all the domains under consideration), and is not over-convoluted (i.e., is not too complex and is sufficiently applicable in practice).

Important Notes:

Numerical Values: The key aspect of this formula is the ability to assign numerical values to the Relevance, Accuracy, Complexity, and Applicability features. This can be achieved through expert judgment, statistical analysis, or other quantitative evaluation methods.

Thresholds: The thresholds (Relevance_Threshold, Accuracy_Threshold, Complexity_Threshold, Applicability_Threshold) must be defined in advance and may depend on the context in which the logic system is used.

Context: The concept of practical validity is always context-dependent. A logic that is practically valid for file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

one domain may be unacceptable for another.

Meta-logical character: The Relevance, Accuracy, Complexity, and Applicability ratings are not part of the logical system L itself. They are meta-logical statements describing properties of L.

Subjectivity: Despite the attempt at formalization, there is inevitably an element of subjectivity in the Relevance, Accuracy, Complexity, and Applicability ratings, especially when setting thresholds.

Practical Application: This formula is more of a conceptual framework than a directly applicable algorithm.

Its value lies in the fact that it emphasizes the need to consider not only the formal validity of logic, but also its applicability and adequacy in specific situations.

This formula, although more complex, is an attempt to formalize the idea of “balance” between simplification and complexity in logic, making it more useful and applicable in the real world (my world). It requires careful definition of all the concepts and numerical parameters used, as well as consideration of the context of application of the logical system.

The world is in the zeroing stage. Logical formula.

In my world, which is in the zeroing stage, “zeroing” is not just the disappearance of everything, but rather the process of returning to a basic, initial state. This state is defined by a minimal set of fundamental laws and constants from which any complexity can potentially arise. Here is a logical formula describing this process:

Definition:

Zeroing(t) ≡ (∀s ∈ S(t): Reduction(s, ФСт) ∧ Transformation(s, Entropy_Max)) Where:

Zeroing(t): A predicate that takes the value “True” if the world is in the zeroing stage at time t, and

“False” otherwise.

t: A moment in time.

S(t): The set of all structures (objects, systems, patterns, etc.) existing in the world at time t. This structure can include anything: physical objects, biological organisms, social institutions, knowledge, laws.

s: A separate structure (an element of the set S(t)).

FS: Fundamental Structure (or Fundamental State). This is the minimum set of basic laws, constants, and elements from which any other structure in the world can be built. FS is considered given and unchangeable.

Reduction(s, FS): A predicate that takes the value “True” if the structure s is reduced (destroyed, simplified) to the state described by the Fundamental Structure FS, and “False” otherwise. This means that the structure s loses its complexity and returns to its basic components and laws.

Entropy_Max: The maximum possible entropy for a given system. Entropy is understood here as a measure of chaos and disorder.

Transformation(s, Entropy_Max): Predicate that takes the value “True” if the structure s is transformed into a state with the maximum possible entropy, and “False” otherwise. This means that the structure s loses its organization and becomes maximally chaotic.

∀: Universal quantifier (for all).

Explanation:

The world is in the stage of zeroing at time t only if for all structures s existing in the world at time t, two conditions are met:

The structure s is reduced (destroyed, simplified) to the state described by the Fundamental Structure FSt.

The structure s is transformed into a state with the maximum possible entropy.

Important consequences and explanations:

FSt as a core: The Fundamental Structure FSt is the core to which everything returns. This could be a set of physical laws, constants, fundamental particles, or some other minimal system capable of generating a universe.

Information Destruction: Nullification implies the loss of information. Information contained in complex structures is erased in the process of reduction and transformation.

Not necessarily complete disappearance: “Nullification” does not necessarily mean the complete cessation of something’s existence. Rather, it is a return to the simplest and most chaotic state from which a new file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

cycle could potentially begin.

Reversibility: The formula does not say whether the process of nullification is reversible. In my world, it could be either a final end or part of a cyclical process.

Examples:

In a physical sense, nullification could mean the return of all matter and energy to the state of singularity from which the universe emerged.

In a biological sense, nullification could mean the disappearance of all living organisms and their decomposition into basic elements.

In a social sense, nullification could mean the destruction of all social institutions and a return to a primitive state.

Entropy as an engine: Increasing entropy plays a key role in the process of zeroing. It is the force that pushes structures towards disorganization and decay.

Time: Zeroing occurs in time. The formula does not describe an instantaneous event, but a process that occurs over a period of time.

The formula does not describe the cause of zeroing, but its state. The formula does not explain why the world is in the zeroing stage. This may be the result of some external forces, internal instabilities, or simply a natural stage in the cycle of the universe's existence.

This logical formula is an attempt to formalize the concept of "zeroing" in my world, focusing on the reduction to basic structures and the increase in entropy. It reflects the idea of a return to the origins, the erasure of complex information and the potential for a new beginning.

Zeroing is limited to the sphere of influence of the creators. Only what they created is zeroed. Worlds created by others remain untouched.

If the World is zeroed, then everything related to it is zeroed, and only the World that is under the power of those who created it will be zeroed. The World that was not created by them will continue to exist -> they can only zero Themselves and what they themselves created.

Definition:

Zeroing(t, M) ≡ Created(M, C) → (∀s ∈ S(t, M): Reduction(s, ФСт(M)) ∧ Transformation(s, Entropy_Max(M)))

Where:

Zeroing(t, M): Predicate that takes the value “True” if the world M is in the process of zeroing at time t, and “False” otherwise.

t: Moment of time.

M: World (a separate space of existence with its own laws and structures).

Created(M, C): Predicate that takes the value “True” if the world M was created by the entity (or entities) C, and “False” otherwise.

C: The entity (or entities) that created the world. This could be God, the Demiurge, a programmer, or any other force capable of creating worlds.

→: Logical implication operator (IF…THEN).

S(t, M): The set of all structures (objects, systems, patterns, etc.) existing in the world M at time t. This structure can include anything: physical objects, biological organisms, social institutions, knowledge, laws, but only within the world M.

s: A separate structure (an element of the set S(t, M)).

ФСт(М): Fundamental Structure (or Fundamental State) for world M. This is the minimum set of basic laws, constants and elements from which any other structure in world M can be built. ФСт is considered given and unchangeable for each specific world. Important: ФСт can be different for different worlds.

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

Reduction(s, ФСт(М)): Predicate that takes the value “True” if structure s is reduced (destroyed, simplified) to the state described by the Fundamental Structure ФСт(М) of world M, and “False”

otherwise.

Entropy_Max(М): Maximum possible entropy for world M. Again, maximum entropy may depend on the laws and constants of a specific world.

Transformation(s, Entropy_Max(M)): Predicate that is True if structure s is transformed into a state with the maximum possible entropy for world M, and False otherwise.

∀: Universal quantifier (for all).

Explanation:

IF world M was created by entity C, THEN world M is in the process of being zeroed out at time t only if the following conditions are met for all structures s existing in world M at time t: Structure s is reduced (destroyed, simplified) to a state described by Fundamental Structure ФСт(M) of world M.

Structure s is transformed into a state with the maximum possible entropy for world M.

Important consequences and clarifications:

Implication: The key element is the implication. The formula does not state that all worlds are zeroed out.

It says that if a world is created, it can be reset (in accordance with the conditions of reduction and transformation).

Limitation of reset: Reset affects only created worlds. If a world is not created by C, the formula says nothing about its state. It can continue to exist as usual, without being reset.

ФСт(М) and Entropy_Max(М) are world-dependent: It is important that the Fundamental Structure and Maximum Entropy are defined for each world separately. This means that different worlds can have different basic laws and different final states.

Authority of the creator: The formula implies that the creator has authority over his creation, including the ability to reset it.

Independence of worlds: Worlds created by different entities are independent of each other in the process of reset. Resetting one world does not affect the existence of others.

Entity C is not necessarily reset: The formula does not say what happens to the entity C itself, which created the world. It can continue to exist and create other worlds, or it can be nullified itself (but this will be described by another formula, related to the world in which C exists).

Exception for uncreated worlds: If ¬Created(M, C), i.e. world M is not created by entity C, then Nullification(t, M) can be true or false regardless of the actions of entity C. In other words, this world can be nullified for its own reasons or not be nullified at all.

Example:

Suppose entity A created world X, and entity B created world Y. If A decides to nullify its world X, then the structures in world X will be reduced to ФСт(X) and transformed to Entropy_Max(X). World Y will remain untouched, and its structures will develop (or collapse) according to its own laws.

This logical formula accurately reflects the given condition: the zeroing is limited only to created worlds and does not affect worlds created by other entities. It emphasizes the creator's power over his creation and the independence of worlds from each other.

Mathematics cannot govern logic because mathematics is a logical algorithm, logic is the rules of correct reasoning - a syllogism. A logical formula for proof.

Okay, I get it. In my world, it is claimed that mathematics cannot govern logic because mathematics is just one of the logical algorithms, and logic is a more fundamental system of rules of correct reasoning (syllogisms). We need to prove this statement using a logical formula.

Definition:

¬(Governs(Mathematics, Logic)) ≡ (Algorithm(Mathematics, Logic) ∧ (∀s ∈ Syllogisms: Logic_Determines(s)) ∧ ¬(Mathematics_Determines(s)))

Where:

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

¬: Logical Negation Operator (NOT).

Governs(Math, Logic): Predicate that is True if Mathematics governs Logic, and False otherwise.

"Governs" here means that Mathematics determines which reasoning in Logic is correct and which is not.

Algorithm(Math, Logic): Predicate that is True if Mathematics is an algorithm based on the principles of Logic, and False otherwise. This means that mathematical operations and reasoning obey logical rules.

Syllogisms: The set of all possible syllogisms (correct forms of logical reasoning). A syllogism is a logical inference consisting of two premises and a conclusion (e.g., "All men are mortal; Socrates is a man; Therefore, Socrates is mortal").

s: An individual syllogism (an element of the set Syllogisms).

Logic_Defines(s): A predicate that evaluates to "True" if the validity of a syllogism s is determined by the rules of Logic (independently of Mathematics), and "False" otherwise.

Mathematics_Defines(s): A predicate that evaluates to "True" if the validity of a syllogism s is determined by the rules of Mathematics, and "False" otherwise.

∀: Universal quantifier (for all).

Explanation:

Mathematics does not control Logic unless the following conditions are met: Mathematics is an algorithm based on the principles of Logic.

For all syllogisms s, the validity of s is determined by the rules of Logic.

For all syllogisms s, the validity of s is not determined by the rules of Mathematics.

Explanation and justification of each condition:

Algorithm(Mathematics, Logic): This states that mathematics is essentially a set of logical operations performed according to certain rules. Mathematical proofs are built on logical axioms and rules of inference. The mathematical operations themselves (addition, multiplication, etc.) can be thought of as logical functions that transform inputs (numbers) into outputs. If mathematics were not governed by logic, then mathematical proofs would be arbitrary and unreliable.

∀s ∈ Syllogisms: Logic_Determines(s): This states that the correctness of any logical reasoning (presented in the form of a syllogism) is determined by the rules of logic itself, not by mathematical principles. Logical rules (e.g. modus ponens, modus tollens, de Morgan's laws) establish which inferences are valid, regardless of whether mathematical symbols and operations are used.

∀s ∈ Syllogisms: ¬(Mathematics_Defines(s)): This is the key statement. It says that although mathematics uses logic, it does not define the rules of logic. Mathematical methods can be applied to analyze logical systems (e.g., using set theory or Boolean algebra), but this does not mean that mathematics defines the correctness of logical reasoning itself. Logical rules exist before and independently of mathematics. If mathematics defined logic, then changing the principles of mathematics would change the laws of logic themselves, which contradicts our intuition about the fundamentality of logical rules. Example: The syllogism “All cats are animals; Murka is a cat; Therefore Murka is an animal”

is true independently of any mathematical theory.

Example Application:

Consider the syllogism: “All numbers divisible by 4 are divisible by 2; 8 is divisible by 4; Therefore, 8 is divisible by 2.”

Logic_Determines(syllogism): The correctness of this syllogism is determined by the logical rule modus ponens (if P → Q, and P is true, then Q is true). The structure of the syllogism corresponds to this rule, which makes it valid.

Mathematics_Determines(syllogism): Although the syllogism uses mathematical concepts (numbers, divisibility), the form of reasoning itself (modus ponens) is not mathematical. Mathematics only confirms the truth of the premises (“All numbers divisible by 4 are divisible by 2” and “8 is divisible by 4”), but does not determine the validity of the logical conclusion itself.

Conclusion:

This logical formula proves that in my world mathematics cannot govern logic, because mathematics is only a logical algorithm that obeys logical rules, and logic is a more fundamental system of rules for correct reasoning that determines the validity of inferences independently of mathematics. Mathematics file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

can use logic, but not define it.

Conflict does not determine the truth and does not solve the problem at its core. ∴ΩΝ

Logical formula

C ⇏ T ∧ C ⇏ S

Where:

C is a conflict,

T is the truth,

S is a solution to the problem,

⇏ is "does not entail",

∧ is a logical "AND".

This formula says that conflict does not determine truth and does not solve the problem.

Logical proof

Conflict does not determine truth (C⇏T) Truth is determined by objective facts, logic and evidence, and not by a struggle of opinions or forces.

In an argument or war, the winner may not be right, but simply stronger or more cunning.

If conflict determined truth, it would depend on force, not on reality, which is logically absurd.

Conflict does not solve the problem at its core (C⇏S) Conflict often exacerbates problems, leading to new destruction and division.

Solving a problem requires analysis, understanding the causes and developing constructive ways out, rather than a simple clash of the parties.

War, for example, rarely leads to a final solution to a problem - it creates new problems and only temporarily suppresses old ones.

Conclusion:

Conflict in itself is not a tool for understanding the truth and does not solve problems, but rather aggravates them.

Violation of the correspondence between the Assign of the elements of the system leads to degeneration of the system and destroys the system.

In this case, we are talking about the discrepancy between how the elements of the system should work (their purposes) and how they actually work.

Statement: Violation of the correspondence between the purposes of the elements of the system leads to degeneration and destruction of the system.

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

1. Logical Formula

Let's define the variables:

N: Violation of correspondence (discrepancy between the intended functions of the element and its actual behavior). Let's call this "functional discrepancy".

S: System (an ordered set of elements that are interconnected).

D: Degeneration of the system (loss of functionality, decrease in efficiency).

R: Destruction of the system (complete cessation of functioning).

Logical Formula:

N → (D ∨ R)

Which reads as: “If there is a functional mismatch between the intended purpose of the elements and their behavior, then this entails degeneration or destruction of the system (or both).”

2. Logical Formula of the Process

Let's add factors that affect the severity of the consequences: I: Intensity of the mismatch (how much the actual behavior of the element deviates from the intended one). This can be interpreted as the degree of the error.

C: Criticality of the element in which the mismatch occurred (the importance of the element for the overall operation of the system).

d: Dependency between system elements (the more an element depends on other elements for correct operation, the more serious the consequences of the mismatch will be).

M: Error detection and correction mechanisms (the presence and effectiveness of monitoring, self-diagnosis, and correction systems).

Process formula:

(N ∧ I ∧ C ∧ d ∧ ¬M) → (D ∨ R)

Which reads as: “If there is a functional mismatch, and it is severe, and it occurs in a critical element, and this element is highly dependent on other elements, and the system lacks mechanisms for detecting and correcting errors, then this entails degeneration or destruction of the system (or both).”

3. Proof (Argumentation)

Violation of the logic of the system: If an element performs a function other than the one for which it is intended, or performs it incorrectly, this violates the logic of the entire system. Example: In a computer program, if the function responsible for sorting data produces an incorrect result (functional mismatch), the entire program may not work correctly and produce erroneous results (degeneration).

Cascading Effect of Errors: If one component malfunctions, it can cause other components that depend on it to malfunction, creating a cascading effect of errors. Example: In a car, if the ABS sensor (the component that controls braking) gives incorrect readings (functional mismatch), it can cause the entire braking system to malfunction, which can lead to an accident (failure).

Unpredictable Behavior: A functional mismatch makes the system's behavior unpredictable. This makes it difficult to diagnose problems and develop effective solutions. Example: In a power plant control system, if the temperature sensors give inaccurate data (functional mismatch), operators will be unable to correctly assess the condition of the equipment and make decisions, which can lead to an accident.

Loss of Trust: If the system frequently fails due to functional mismatches, users will lose trust in the system and stop using it. Example: An online store that frequently experiences errors when processing orders (functional mismatch) will quickly lose customers.

Increased Maintenance Costs: Functional inconsistencies can be expensive to detect and correct, especially if they are difficult to diagnose. Example: In a complex industrial installation, finding and correcting a fault in the logic of one of the controllers (a functional inconsistency) can be time-consuming and resource-intensive.

Counterarguments and Limitations:

Redundancy: In systems with redundancy, if one element stops working correctly, another element can file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

take over its function.

Self-healing: Some systems have the ability to automatically detect and correct errors.

Limited Impact: Not all functional inconsistencies are equally important. Some may have only a minor impact on the overall operation of the system.

System Adaptation: In some cases, the system can adapt to a functional inconsistency by changing the way the elements interact.

Conclusion:

Functional mismatch between the purpose of system elements and their actual behavior is a serious threat to the system's operability. The intensity, criticality of the element, dependency between elements, and lack of error correction mechanisms aggravate the negative consequences. To ensure reliable operation of the system, it is necessary to carefully design the system, conduct regular checks, and have error detection and correction mechanisms. It is also important to design the system so that the system can adapt to changes.

Complexity: “…because of its complexity…”. This emphasizes that Complexity is a characteristic of the crisis itself, making it difficult to resolve. This is important!

Irresistible: “…which leads to an insurmountable catastrophe.” Here “Irresistible” refers to the fact that the transition to a catastrophe cannot be prevented due to the accumulated problems and the complexity of the situation. That is, Irresistible is not the crisis itself (as I suggested earlier), but the trajectory of movement to a catastrophe.

(D↑ ∧ Inevitable): “irresistible catastrophe”. Here D↑ is the increase in catastrophicity, and Inevitable is its inevitability (as expected).

“If the decline in intelligence leads to the complexity of systems, then (if the crisis, becoming both intractable and inevitable, leads to the complexity of its structure), then (if the trajectory to disaster becomes irresistible), then the catastrophe inevitably approaches with increasing force.”

I know that I know nothing. A logical formula.

Okay, this is a classic philosophical statement attributed to Socrates. Representing it as a logical formula is a bit more difficult, since it requires defining what it means to “know” and “nothing.” Here are a few options, with different levels of formality and assumptions: Option 1 (The simplest, but least precise):

Let Z(x, y) mean “x knows y.”

Let ¬ mean “not.”

Let knowledge be the general concept of knowledge.

The formula would then be:

¬Z(I, knowledge)

Which reads: “I don’t know knowledge.” This is, of course, very simplified and doesn’t capture the nuances. It simply denies that I have knowledge at all.

Option 2 (More precise, using a quantifier):

Let Z(x, y) mean “x knows y.”

Let Ω denote the set of all things that can be known (the domain of knowledge).

Let ∀y mean “for all y”.

Then the formula will be:

∀y (y ∈ Ω → ¬Z(Я, y))

Which is read: “For all y, if y belongs to the set of all things that can be known, then I do not know y.”

This means that I do not know anything that can be known.

Option 3 (Reflexive, reflects self-awareness):

This option tries to reflect the fact that I know about my ignorance: Z(x, y): “x knows y”.

¬Z(x, y): “x does not know y”.

Then the formula is:

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

Z(Я, (∀y (y ∈ Ω → ¬Z(Я, y))))

What is read: “I know that for all y, if y belongs to the set of all things that can be known, then I do not know y.” This means that I know about my general ignorance. This is the most accurate formalization, conveying the essence of Socrates' statement.

Which option should I choose?

Option 1 is good for brevity, but greatly simplifies.

Option 2 better conveys the meaning of “not knowing anything”, covering all possible knowledge.

Option 3 is the best, as it reflects the reflexivity of the statement - knowledge about ignorance. It best corresponds to the philosophical meaning of Socrates' statement.

Let's rework the statements and formulas, replacing “knowledge” with “life”. This will add a different shade of meaning.

1. Statement based on the formula: ¬Z(Me, life) (Simplest version): Formula: ¬Z(Me, life) (Now Z(x, y) means “x knows/understands y”) Statement: “I don’t understand life.” Or, “I don’t know life.” (Reflects a lack of understanding of the fundamental principles of life).

2. Statement based on the formula: ∀y (y ∈ Ω → ¬Z(Me, y)) (A more general version, where Ω is all aspects of life):

Formula: ∀y (y ∈ Ω → ¬Z(Me, y)) (where Ω is now “the set of all aspects of life, experiences, meanings”) Statement: “I don’t understand anything that makes up life.” Or, “Everything that exists in life is incomprehensible to me.” (Reflects a total lack of understanding of life situations, feelings, goals).

3. Statement based on the formula: Z(Я, (∀y (y ∈ Ω → ¬Z(Я, y)))) (Reflexive option): Formula: Z(Я, (∀y (y ∈ Ω → ¬Z(Я, y)))

Statement: “I understand that I do not understand life.” Or: “I realize that I do not know what life is in all its manifestations.” (Reflects a conscious lack of understanding of life, recognition of one’s inability to comprehend its meaning and complexity).

Analysis of each option with a new context:

First option: The simplest. Denies understanding of life as a whole. May mean that a person feels detached from life or does not understand its goals.

Second option: Broader. Speaks of a complete lack of understanding of all aspects of life. This may reflect a deep sense of alienation, depression, or spiritual crisis.

Option 3: The most complex and interesting. It indicates that a person is aware of their lack of understanding of life. This may be a starting point for finding meaning, spiritual growth, or changing their attitude toward the world. Here hope appears, since awareness of a problem is the first step toward solving it.
