What happens if the World Authority ignores arguments that are greater than zero? Logical model, logical proof and logical formula.

Let's build a logical model of the situation where the World Authority (WA) ignores arguments that are greater than zero. We will assume that the "argument" (A) is a sentence with some weight (w), expressed by a number. Weight can be positive (supporting) or negative (contradictory). WA makes a decision (P) based on the arguments.

1. Logical model:

Ai: the i-th argument (i = 1, 2, …, n)

wi: the weight of the argument Ai (wi ∈ ℝ)

WA(A1, A2, …, An): a function describing the WA's decision based on the arguments.

P: WM decision (e.g. accept/reject a proposal)

The condition of ignoring arguments with weight > 0 can be written as follows: If wi > 0, then WM(A1, A2, …, An) does not depend on Ai.

2. Logical proof (informal):

Suppose there is a set of arguments {A1, A2, …, An} with weights {w1, w2, …, wn}. If WM ignores all arguments with wi > 0, then the WM decision will depend only on arguments with wi ≤ 0. This means that potentially important information (arguments with positive weight) is completely ignored, which will lead to irrational and potentially catastrophic decisions. For example, if there is a strong argument in favor of preventing a global catastrophe (wi >> 0), but WM ignores it, then a catastrophe may occur.

3. Logical formula (simple version):

Introduce the predicate I(Ai) — “MV ignores argument Ai”. Then the condition can be written as:

∀i (wi > 0 → I(Ai))

This formula states that for all i, if the weight of the i-th argument is greater than zero, then MV ignores this argument.

A more complex formula that takes into account decision making: We need to introduce a decision function R(A1,…,An), which returns a true value if the decision is made, and a false value if not. Then:

∀A1,…,An ∀i (wi > 0 → (R(A1,…,An) ↔ R(A1,…,Ai-1, Ai+1,…,An))) This means that the result of the decision (R) does not change if we remove any argument with positive weight.

Conclusion:

Ignoring arguments with positive weighting leads to incomplete information on which the World Authority bases its decision. This, in turn, increases the likelihood of making ineffective, irrational and potentially dangerous decisions for the Authority itself, since important information is ignored. This is a logically unsound decision-making strategy.

The probability of winning a war is that soldiers with an IQ higher than the enemy's.

I propose a logical model that relates the probability of winning a war to the difference in average IQ of soldiers on both sides. It is important to remember that this is a simplified model and many other factors influence the actual outcome of a war, such as:

Number of soldiers

Technological superiority

Logistics

Motivation

Strategic planning

Leadership

Resources

Geographical location

and many more. This model focuses exclusively on one factor - the difference in IQ - to illustrate the logical relationship.

Logical Model:

Premise 1 (P1): Higher average IQ of soldiers is correlated with better strategic planning, tactical execution, adaptation to changing circumstances, use of innovation, and decision making under stress.

Premise 2 (P2): Better strategic planning, tactical execution, adaptation, innovation, and decision making increase the probability of achieving military objectives.

Premise 3 (P3): Achieving military objectives is a necessary (but not sufficient) condition for winning the war.

Logical Proof:

From P1 and P2 it follows: A higher average IQ of soldiers is correlated with an increased probability of achieving military objectives.

From P3 it follows: An increased probability of achieving military objectives increases the chances of winning the war.

Therefore: A higher average IQ of soldiers increases the probability of winning the war.

Logical Formula:

Define the variables:

P(W): The probability of winning the war.

IQ_A: The average IQ of soldiers of side A (the attacker).

IQ_D: The average IQ of soldiers of side D (the defender).

ΔIQ = IQ_A - IQ_D: The difference in average IQ between sides A and D.

f(x): A function mapping the difference in IQ to the probability of winning.

Then the formula is:

P(W) = f(ΔIQ)

Where f(x) is an increasing function. This means that the larger the ΔIQ (IQ difference), the higher the P(W) (the probability of winning).

An example of a function f(x) (linear):

f(x) = 0.5 + k * x

where:

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

0.5 - Base probability of winning with equal IQ (50%).

k - Coefficient that determines the effect of the difference in IQ on the probability of winning. k should be small enough to keep P(W) between 0 and 1. For example, if IQ is measured in common units, k might be 0.01 (that is, each point of difference in IQ increases the probability of winning by 1%).

An example of a function f(x) (sigmoid):

A sigmoid function may be more realistic, since it shows diminishing returns to increasing IQ. For a very large difference in IQ, the probability of winning will not increase linearly, but will tend to 1.

f(x) = 1 / (1 + exp(-k * x))

where:

k - The coefficient that determines the slope of the sigmoid and, therefore, the rate of change in the probability of winning depending on the difference in IQ.

It is important to note:

The value of k (the coefficient) in both formulas is a model parameter that must be determined empirically (based on historical data or expert estimates).

This formula is just one of the possible ways to model the effect of IQ on the probability of winning. More complex models can take into account other factors and their interaction with IQ.

Mermaid diagram:

graph LR

A[IQ_A: Average IQ of side A] --> C{ΔIQ = IQ_A - IQ_D};

B[IQ_D: Average IQ of side D] --> C;

C --> D[f(ΔIQ): Probability of victory versus ΔIQ];

D --> E{P(W): Probability of victory for side A};

style A fill:#f9f,stroke:#333,stroke-width:2px

style B fill:#f9f,stroke:#333,stroke-width:2px

style C fill:#ccf,stroke:#333,stroke-width:2px

style D fill:#ccf,stroke:#333,stroke-width:2px

style E fill:#afa,stroke:#333,stroke-width:2px

mermaid

Explanation of the diagram:

A: Input: Average IQ of army A.

B: Input: Average IQ of army D.

C: Calculate the difference in IQ (ΔIQ = IQ_A - IQ_D).

D: Applying the function f(ΔIQ), which converts the difference in IQ into the probability of winning.

E: Output: The probability of victory of army A ( P(W)).

Key Takeaways:

This model shows how the difference in the average IQ of soldiers can be logically related to the probability of winning a war. However, I emphasize that this is a greatly simplified model. In reality, many other factors influence the outcome of a war, which can either strengthen or weaken the effect of IQ.

This model is intended to illustrate the logical relationship, not to accurately predict the outcome of real conflicts.

Do not take the name of the Lord in vain - Do not ask the Lord God for what you can do yourself.

Analysis of the statement “Do not take the name of the Lord in vain - Do not ask the Lord God for what you can do yourself”

This statement can be seen as a call for a rational approach to life and faith. It warns against unreasonable expectations of miracles and calls for relying on your own strength when possible.

Logical model:

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

You can present the logical model as a rule: Condition: You can do something yourself (have the ability, resources, opportunity).

Consequence: Do not ask God for help in this.

Logical proof (based on assumptions):

Assumption 1 (Presumption of expediency): Actions should be aimed at achieving the goal in the most effective way.

Assumption 2 (Presumption of limited resources): Resources (including time, energy and the “attention”

of higher powers) are limited.

Assumption 3 (Presumption of rationality of higher powers): Higher powers distribute resources rationally, supporting those who cannot help themselves.

Argument: If you can achieve your goal yourself, then turning to higher powers is not the most effective way, because: It can divert limited resources from those who really need help.

This can be regarded as irrational use of the “attention” of higher powers.

Conclusion: Therefore, if you can do something yourself, do not ask God for help in this.

Logical formula (preliminary, requiring clarification of the context): Let's denote:

P: You can do X yourself.

Q: You ask God for help in X.

Then the formula can look like this:

P -> ¬Q (If P is true, then Q is false).

Or, expressing the same logic through quantifiers (it will be necessary to clarify whether it is necessary to understand “always” or “often”):

∀X (You_Can_Do_It_Yourself(You, X) -> ¬Ask_God(You, Help_In_X)) What is read: “For any X, if you can do X yourself, then you do not ask God for help in X.”

It is important to note:

This logical model simplifies a complex religious and philosophical statement. It focuses on the rational aspect, ignoring, for example, issues of humility, prayer as a way of communicating with God, and other nuances of faith.

The formula needs to be clarified in context. For example, even if a person can do something himself, he can still ask God for strength and wisdom to do it. In this case, the formula should be more complex, taking into account the goals and motives of the person asking.

The very concept of “you can do it yourself” also requires clarification. Is everything that is theoretically possible considered something you “can do yourself”?

In conclusion, the statement calls for a balance between faith and rationality, calling for responsibility and active participation in one’s own life, without leaving everything to chance or higher powers. However, the interpretation and application of this statement depends on personal beliefs and the context of the situation.

combinations a + combinations b -> combinations c Three explanations of God's Law: Do not harm anyone who does not harm you, Be loyal to anyone who is loyal to you, Be honest and respectful to anyone who is honest and respectful to you. + Treat people the way you want to be treated.

Logical model of God's Law (with three explanations):

1. Do not harm anyone who does not harm you:

Model: Mutual non-aggression. If the other person does not show aggression, then you should not show aggression.

Proof: Aggression provokes counter-aggression, creating a cycle of violence. Non-aggressive behavior, on the contrary, promotes peace and security.

Formula: ¬Harm(B) → ¬Harm(A), where: A - you (subject)

B - another person (object)

Harm(X) - predicate “X causes harm”

¬ - logical negation (“not”)

→ - logical implication (“if…then”)

Read: “If another does not harm you, then do not harm him.”

2. Be faithful to the one who is faithful to you:

Model: Mutual loyalty and support. Loyalty for loyalty.

Proof: Loyalty strengthens relationships, trust and cooperation. Betrayal destroys connections.

Formula: Ver(B) → Ver(A), where: A - you (subject)

B - another person (object)

Ver(X) - predicate “X is true”

→ - logical implication (“if…then”)

Read: “If another is true to you, then be true to him.”

3. Be honest and respectful to those who are honest and respectful to you: Model: Mutual honesty and respect. Reflection of partner’s behavior.

Proof: Honesty and respect create a favorable atmosphere for communication and cooperation.

Dishonesty and disrespect destroy trust.

Formula: (Honest(B) ∧ Respects(B)) → (Honest(A) ∧ Respects(A)), where:A - you (subject) B - another person (object)

Honest(X) - predicate “X is honest”

Respects(X) - predicate “X respects”

∧ - logical “and”

→ - logical implication (“if…then”)

Read: “If the other is honest and respectful to you, then you too be honest and respectful to him.”

4. Treat people the way you want them to treat you:

Model: The Golden Rule of Ethics. Reflecting your own desires in relation to others.

Proof: This rule promotes harmonious relationships, as it implies empathy and consideration of the needs of others.

Formula: Want(Relation(B, A)) → Treat(A, B), where: A - you (subject) B - another person (object)

Want(X) - predicate “you want X to be”

Relation(X, Y) - predicate “X treats Y”

→ - logical implication (“if…then”)

Treat(A, B) - predicate “You treat B”

Read: “If you want B to treat you in a certain way, then you treat B in the same way.”

General logical model:

All these formulas are based on the principle of reciprocity: the action of one subject (A) depends on the action of another subject (B). They describe cause-and-effect relationships in social interactions, emphasizing the importance of benevolent and fair behavior. Their observance contributes to harmonious relationships and the common good.

Word to the World: If they die, then humanity will no longer exist as a species.

Word to the World: If they die, humanity will cease to exist as a species.

Who are they?

This question has no clear answer without additional context. However, given the phrase “if they die, humanity will cease to exist as a species,” we can assume that we are talking about: People with critical knowledge and skills for survival: This could be a small group of scientists, engineers, doctors, agronomists, or people who know the secrets of survival in extreme conditions.

A specific group of people capable of reproduction and maintaining genetic diversity: If genetic diversity is reduced to a critical level, the species may not be able to adapt to changing conditions and will die out.

Representatives of each social, cultural, religious, and geographic group that together make up the diversity of humanity. This is important for adaptability, sustainability, and development.

Logical Model:

Premise 1 (P1): There is a group “X.”

Premise 2 (P2): If group “X” dies, then humanity as a species ceases to exist.

Conclusion (C): Therefore, group “X” is critical to the survival of humanity.

Logical proof (modus ponens):

P → Q: If group “X” dies (P), then humanity as a species will cease to exist (Q).

P: Group “X” dies (hypothetically).

Q: Therefore, humanity as a species will cease to exist.

This proof shows that if the premise of group “X” is true, then the conclusion of humanity’s demise is inevitable.

Logical formula:

Let:

D(X) = Group “X” dies

E = Humanity exists as a species

Then the statement can be represented as:

D(X) → ¬E

Where:

→ - implication (if…then…)

¬ - negation (not)

Interpretation of the formula: If group “X” dies, then it is not true that humanity exists as a species.

It is important to note: This logical model and formula are simplifications. In reality, the survival of humanity depends on many interrelated factors, and the death of even a critical group of people will not necessarily lead to the immediate extinction of the species, but can seriously undermine its chances of survival in the long term.

As long as there is Life, it will be; When it is no longer there, it will no longer be. As long as there is, it will be; When it is no longer there, it will no longer be. This is irreversible.

Logical analysis of the statements presented:

Statements:

As long as there is Life, it will be; When it is no longer there, it will no longer be.

As long as there is, it will be; When it is no longer there, it will no longer be.

This is irreversible.

Logical model:

These statements can be represented as a logical model using time as a basis: t0: Initial moment of time.

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

t1: Subsequent moment of time (t1 > t0).

Life(t): Function denoting the presence of Life at time t.

Existence(t): Function denoting the presence of “something” (generalized existence) at time t.

Statement 1 (Life):

If Life(t0) is true, then Life(t1) can be true or false. However, if there is a time t* such that Life(t*) is false, then for all t > t*, Life(t) will be false. This can be expressed as:

∀ t: (Life(t) -> ∃ t' > t: Life(t')) ∨ (∃ t*: Life(t*) = False -> ∀ t > t*: Life(t) = False) Proposition 2 (Existence):

Similar to the first proposition, but more general.

∀ t: (Existence(t) -> ∃ t' > t: Existence(t')) ∨ (∃ t*: Existence(t*) = False -> ∀ t > t*: Existence(t) =

False)

Claim 3 (Irreversibility):

This is the key claim. It implies that once something has ceased to exist, it cannot return to existence.

Formally:

¬∃ t1 > t0: (¬Life(t0) ∧ Life(t1)) (for Life) ¬∃ t1 > t0: (¬Existence(t0) ∧ Existence(t1)) (for Existence) Logical Proof:

The proof here is more of a demonstration that the claims agree with basic principles of logic and intuition. The statements are based on the principles of:

Causality: The existence of something at a given moment in time is a condition for its existence in the future. (If there is no cause, then there is no effect).

Finity: The model assumes that the resources and conditions for the existence of something are limited.

Irreversibility of time (in this context): Once something has ceased to exist, it cannot be brought back into existence. (This is an assumption that may be challenged in some physical or philosophical contexts).

Logical formula (a more formalized representation):

Using temporal logic (the logic of time), suppose that F means “at some point in the future”. Then: G(Life -> F Life) ∧ (¬Life -> G ¬Life) ∧ G(Existence -> F Existence) ∧ (¬Existence -> G ¬Existence) Where:

G means “always in the future”.

Life and Existence are propositional variables denoting the presence of Life and Existence respectively.

-> means “implies”.

¬ means “does not”.

F means “at some point in the future”.

This formula expresses: “Always in the future, if there is Life, then at some point in the future Life will exist. And always in the future, if there is no Life, then there will be no Life”. A similar statement for Existence.

Conclusion:

The statements presented express basic intuitive concepts about existence and irreversibility. They can be formalized using logical models and formulas, although the accuracy and completeness of the formalization depend on the level of detail and assumptions adopted in the model. The key assumption here is irreversibility, which is an assumption about the nature of time and cause-and-effect relationships.

Russia is the Center of Balance between East and West, any action against Russia will be irreversible.

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

The statement “Russia is the Center of Balance between East and West, any action against Russia will be irreversible” is a rather complex and controversial statement. In order to analyze it logically, it is necessary to break it down into its component parts and consider each statement separately.

Breaking it down into parts:

“Russia is the Center of Balance between East and West”: This is a statement about Russia’s role in the geopolitical landscape.

“Any action against Russia will be irreversible”: This is a statement about the consequences of actions directed against Russia.

Logical Model:

The proposed model is based on assumptions that must be taken into account.

Assumption 1: Russia has a unique geopolitical position linking East and West. This position may be due to geography, history, culture, economy, and/or military power.

Assumption 2: Actions towards Russia have a significant impact on global stability and the balance of power.

Assumption 3: In the context of “actions”, we mean actions aimed at a significant change in the status, influence, territory, or political structure of Russia. Minor, insignificant actions are not considered.

Assumption 4: “Irreversibility” means that the consequences of these actions cannot be completely eliminated or reversed in the foreseeable future.

Logical Proof (Attempt):

Attempt to build a logical proof based on these assumptions: Premise 1 (Assumption 1): Russia is the center of the balance between East and West. (R = C(E,W)) Premise 2 (Assumption 2): Actions towards Russia affect global stability and the balance of power. (A(R) -

> I(G))

Premise 3 (Assumption 3): “Actions” imply significant changes.

Premise 4: “Irreversibility” implies long-term and irreversible consequences.

Argument: If Russia is the center of balance (Premise 1), and actions towards it affect global stability (Premise 2), then any significant action (Premise 3) will upset this balance and will entail long-term and irreversible consequences (Premise 4). Therefore, any action towards Russia will be irreversible.

Logical formula (attempt):

Let:

R - Russia

C(E, W) - Center of balance of East and West

A(R) - Actions towards Russia

I(G) - Impact on global stability

U(X) - Irreversibility of consequences of X

Then:

R = C(E, W) ∧ A(R) -> I(G) => A(R) -> U(A(R))

Which means: If Russia is the center of balance of East and West, and actions towards Russia affect global stability, then actions towards Russia will lead to irreversible consequences.

Criticism and Limitations:

Ambiguous terms: “Center of balance,” “action,” “irreversible” - all these terms need a clear definition.

What exactly is meant by each of them? Without clear definitions, the statement remains too general and is subject to various interpretations.

Controversial Assumptions: Each of the assumptions can be challenged. For example, is Russia really the only center of balance? Or are there other countries that play a similar role? Not all actions towards Russia automatically lead to irreversible consequences. History knows examples when the consequences of certain actions were mitigated or overcome.

Need for Empirical Data: A logical model does not prove the truth of a statement by itself. This requires empirical data confirming Russia's influence on the global balance and the irreversibility of the file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

consequences of actions directed against it. History provides both examples that support this statement and examples that contradict it.

Simplifying Reality: The real world is much more complex than any logical model. Geopolitics is a dynamic process influenced by many factors.

Post hoc ergo propter hoc logical fallacy: It is important to avoid the logical fallacy “after this, therefore because of this.” If significant changes occurred towards Russia after some action, this does not necessarily mean that the action was the only cause of these changes. Conclusion: The statement “Russia is the center of balance between East and West, any action towards Russia will be irreversible” is controversial and requires further verification. The proposed logical model only formalizes the argument, but does not prove its truth. Clear definitions of terms, confirmation of assumptions with empirical data and consideration of the complexity of geopolitical reality are needed. The statement is more of a political statement than a strictly logical conclusion. It reflects a certain vision of Russia's role in the world and warns against ill-considered actions towards it.

Heron's Golden Rule: Do not do your actions to your own detriment.

Heron's Golden Rule and its relationship to the Law of God and the Golden Rule of Christ Heron's Golden Rule, "Do not do your actions to your own detriment," is a form of egoistic ethical principle. Unlike altruistic formulations of the Golden Rule, it focuses on self-preservation and the avoidance of negative consequences for oneself.

Relation to the Law of God: The Law of God, as presented in various religious traditions (e.g., the Ten Commandments in Judaism and Christianity), often includes commandments that coincide with Heron's principle. For example, prohibitions against murder, stealing, or lying often result in negative consequences for the offender. However, the Law of God is broader, including commandments that may require self-sacrifice and actions that do not directly benefit the person (e.g., charity to one's neighbor).

Thus, Heron’s Golden Rule is only a part of God’s Law, not its full equivalent.

Relation to Christ’s Golden Rule: Christ’s Golden Rule (“So whatever you want others to do to you, do also to them,” Matt. 7:12) is an altruistic principle. It focuses on acting toward others rather than avoiding harm to oneself. Although there is a superficial similarity—avoiding harm to others indirectly protects oneself from potential retaliation or negative social consequences—the underlying emphasis is different.

Christ’s Rule requires active compassion and concern for others, even when it requires sacrifice.

Logical Model:

We can represent these three principles as a logical model using propositional logic: H: Do not act in a way that harms you (Heron’s Golden Rule).

B: Obey God’s Law.

C: Do unto others as you would have them do unto you (Christ’s Golden Rule).

It can be argued that H is a necessary but not sufficient condition for B and C: H → B (partial): Part of God's Law is the same as Heron's Rule, but God's Law includes much more.

H → C (partial): Avoiding harm to oneself can indirectly avoid harm to others (e.g., by avoiding aggression), but Christ's Rule requires much more than simply avoiding harm.

Logical Proof (Informal):

We can show that H is not a sufficient condition for B and C by examples. A person can follow Heron's Rule by avoiding actions that would harm him, but still violate God's Law, such as by refusing to help those in need. Similarly, a person can follow Heron's Rule but not follow Christ's Rule by ignoring the needs of others if doing so does not directly cause him problems.

Logical formula (limited):

A strict formal logical formula for displaying the relationships is difficult due to the semantic ambiguity of the concepts of “harm” and “God’s Law”. However, an approximate formula can be proposed using the file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

implication:

H ∧ X → B (partially), where X is a set of other conditions necessary for keeping God’s Law.

H ∧ Y → C (partially), where Y is a set of other conditions necessary for keeping Christ’s Golden Rule.

These formulas show that Heron’s Golden Rule is only one of the factors influencing the observance of God’s Law and Christ’s Golden Rule, but is not the determining factor.

In conclusion, Heron’s Golden Rule is a narrower principle than God’s Law and Christ’s Golden Rule.

Although it may overlap with them in some respects, it does not cover their fullness and moral depth.

Not wanting to die is not a reason for living (an ontological principle), so if you are Alive by the Will of God, then there must be a reason that gives you the right to violate this ontological principle.

Your idea is an interesting philosophical argument that can be broken down and analyzed from a logical point of view.

Breakdown (Premises and Conclusion):

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

Premise 1: Not wanting to die is not a sufficient reason for living (an ontological principle).

Premise 2: If you are alive by the Will of God, then there is a reason that gives you the right to violate the ontological principle (i.e. to live, despite the fact that not wanting to die is not a sufficient reason in itself).

Conclusion (implicit): Therefore, if you are alive by the Will of God, then there is a reason for your life other than simply not wanting to die.

Logical model:

P: Not wanting to die is a reason for living.

¬P: Not wanting to die is not a reason for living (ontological principle).

Q: You are alive by the will of God.

R: There is a reason that gives you the right to violate the ontological principle (i.e., to live).

Logical proof (formal):

¬P (Premise 1)

Q → R (Premise 2)

Q (Assumption - if you are alive by the will of God)

R (Modus Ponens, from 2 and 3)

Therefore, R (There is a reason that gives you the right to violate the ontological principle).

Logical formula:

¬P ∧ (Q → R) ∧ Q ⊢ R

Where:

¬ - negation (“not”)

∧ - conjunction (“and”)

→ - implication (“if… then…”)

⊢ - logical consequence (“it follows from this”)

Analysis and Criticism:

Ontological principle: The claim that not wanting to die is not enough to justify life is a strong claim. Many philosophers believe that life itself is valuable, and the desire to live is a sufficient reason for its continuation.

“The will of God”: This concept presupposes belief in God and His will. From a logical point of view, this requires proof of the existence of God and His will, which is a matter of faith, not logical proof. Without this, the statement Q has no basis.

Violation of the ontological principle: The very concept of “violation” of the ontological principle is not entirely clear. The ontological principle, in this context, is more of a philosophical statement than a law of nature that can be violated.

Reason for Living: The statement that the “Will of God” is a reason for living, other than not wanting to die, opens the question of what that reason is. This requires further explanation.

Conclusion:

Your reasoning is logically consistent if we accept your premises (especially the belief in God and His will).

However, the premises themselves require deeper justification and analysis, especially from a philosophical and ethical perspective. A critical look at the ontological principle, an understanding of the

“will of God” and the nature of the “reason” for living are key to evaluating this logical model.

3. Logical Formula (updated):

Introduce a new variable: Unipolarity

P(Unipolarity | Russia's_Problems) = P(West | Russia's_Problems) + P(China | Russia's_Problems) P(Catastrophe | Unipolarity) = x (where x is the probability of a catastrophe given unipolarity, which needs to be determined)

Let's assume that the probability of a catastrophe under a unipolar world is very high, say 80%: P(Catastrophe | Unipolarity) = 0.8

Then the overall probability of a catastrophe will depend on the probability of unipolarity: P(Catastrophe) = P(Catastrophe | Unipolarity) * P(Unipolarity | Russia's Problems) file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

P(Catastrophe) = 0.8 * (0.333 + 0.333) = 0.8 * 0.666 = 0.533 (or 53.3%) Now we can update the outcome probabilities, given that a unipolar world is a trigger for a catastrophe, and the probability of a catastrophe under unipolarity is 80%. To correctly calculate the final probabilities, more complex modeling is needed, taking into account that a catastrophe caused by unipolarity “cancels”

the unipolar world itself (i.e., if a catastrophe occurs, then the unipolar world no longer exists).

P(China became unipolar and there is no catastrophe) = 0.333 * (1 - 0.8) = 0.333 * 0.2 = 0.0666

P(Catastrophe occurred) = 1 - 0.0666 - 0.0666 = 0.8668 (or 86.68%) Adjusted final probabilities:

P(West became unipolar and there is no catastrophe) = 6.66%

P(China became unipolar and there is no catastrophe) = 6.66%

P(Catastrophe occurred) = 86.68%

Cognition is the most noble activity of man: theoretical reason, comprehending truth, comprehends absolute truth, that is, God.

The statements presented reflect the classical rationalistic approach to the cognition of truth, characteristic of philosophy, especially metaphysics and theology. Let us analyze each position and connect it with the corresponding concepts:

The source of comprehension of Truth is correct reasoning. This statement is the cornerstone of rationalism. It suggests that truth is not revealed through feelings, intuition or revelation, but is achieved through logically consistent and well-founded thinking. The key word here is “correct”. Correctness implies compliance with the laws of logic, the absence of contradictions and the validity of premises. This, in turn, raises questions about what is considered “correct”, who determines the criteria for correctness and how we can be sure of the infallibility of our reasoning.

**Cognition is the mind aimed at comprehending general principles and laws, and not at practical application. It is argued that through comprehension of particular truths one can come to the Absolute Truth, which is identified with God. This is related to the idea of God as the first cause of all things, the source of all truth and perfection. Understanding the truth thus becomes the path to knowledge of God and, consequently, to spiritual perfection. This statement is closely related to the ontological argument for the existence of God.

Logical model, logical proof and logical formula. These concepts are tools of “correct reasoning”.

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

Logical model: It is a formalized representation of a subject area or problem, expressed in the language of logic. It allows one to abstract from specific details and focus on logical connections and relationships.

Examples of logical models: truth tables, Venn diagrams, formal systems.

Logical proof: It is a sequence of logical inferences aimed at substantiating the truth of a certain statement (theorem) based on previously proven statements (axioms or lemmas) and rules of inference.

A strict logical proof guarantees the truth of the conclusion if the initial premises are true.

Logical formula: This is an expression built from logical variables, constants and operations (logical connectives, quantifiers). Logical formulas are used to represent logical statements and relations in a formalized form.

In general, the presented statements form a coherent system based on the following principles: Primacy of reason: Reason is the main instrument for cognition of truth.

Logic as a method: The use of logic is a prerequisite for achieving true knowledge.

Hierarchy of truth: There is a hierarchy of truths leading to the Absolute Truth (God).

Cognition as a spiritual path: Comprehension of truth is seen as a noble activity leading to spiritual perfection.

Critical remarks:

Limitations of rationalism: Critics of rationalism point out that reason can be subject to errors and prejudices. In addition, there are aspects of human experience (e.g. emotions, intuition, faith) that cannot be fully explained and covered by logical analysis.

The problem of justifying axioms: Logical proofs rely on axioms that are accepted without proof. The question of how to justify axioms remains complex and controversial.

Different conceptions of God: Identifying the Absolute Truth with God presupposes a certain understanding of God that may not be shared by everyone.

In conclusion, the statements presented reflect a certain philosophical position that emphasizes the role of reason and logic in knowing the truth. Although this position has its strengths, it is also subject to criticism and must be considered in the context of other approaches to knowledge.

An irreversible action on the foundations of exist is always fatal. Logical model, logical proof and logical formula.

You are right, an irreversible action affecting the foundations of existence is indeed always fatal. This can be expressed through a logical model, proof and formula.

Logical Model:

Consider a system where:

A: Foundations of existence (e.g. fundamental physical laws, biological imperatives, social structures necessary for survival).

B: Irreversible action.

C: Affecting the foundations of existence.

D: Fatality (death of the system, cessation of existence).

The model can be represented as follows:

If B, then C: If an irreversible action occurs, then it affects the foundations of existence. (B -> C) If C, then D: If the foundations of existence are affected, then fatality occurs. (C -> D) So if B, then D: Therefore, if an irreversible action occurs, then fatality occurs. (B -> D) Logical Proof (Modus Ponens):

Premise 1: B -> C (If irreversible action, then fundamentals are involved) Premise 2: C -> D (If fundamentals are involved, then fatality) Conclusion: B -> D (If irreversible action, then fatality) Formal Logical Formula (Propositional Logic):

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

(B -> C) ∧ (C -> D) => (B -> D) Where:

-> stands for “if… then…” (implication)

∧ stands for “and” (conjunction)

=> stands for “therefore”

Explanation:

This model, proof, and formula show that if we accept that irreversible actions do involve fundamentals of existence, and that involving fundamentals of existence does lead to fatality, then logically it follows that irreversible actions are always fatal.

Important Points:

Defining “Existence Fundamentals”: It is key to clearly define what is meant by “existence fundamentals.”

This may be different in different contexts (physical, biological, social, etc.).

Degree of Impact: Not every impact on existence fundamentals is necessarily fatal. What matters is the degree of impact. If the changes are minor and the system can adapt to them, then fatality may not occur. However, if the impact is irreversible and undermines the fundamental principles of the system’s functioning, then fatality becomes highly probable.

Capacity for Recovery/Adaptation: In the real world, systems often have mechanisms for recovery and adaptation. If these mechanisms are able to compensate for the impact of an irreversible action, then fatality may be averted. However, if the action is truly irreversible and exceeds the ability to adapt, then the logic above applies.

Context: It is important to consider the context in which the statement is made. For example, for an individual organism, irreversible DNA damage can be fatal. For an ecosystem, irreversible pollution of a key resource can lead to its collapse. For all of humanity, irreversible climate change (exceeding critical points of no return) can have catastrophic consequences.

In conclusion, the proposed logical model and proof correctly reflect the relationship between irreversible actions, the basis of existence, and fatality, provided that we accept the initial assumptions about irreversibility and the significance of the impact.

Zones of Natural Conditions of Existence and Species (Genetic) Diversity are Required. Logical Model, Logical Proof, and Logical Formula. Mermaid Diagram.

Zones of Natural Conditions of Existence and Species (Genetic) Diversity: Logical Analysis 1. Statement Breakdown:

Thesis: Zones of Natural Conditions of Existence and Species (Genetic) Diversity are Required.

Definition of Zones: Special territories designated for the conservation of natural ecosystems and genetic diversity.

Justification: The need to conserve species and maintain the sustainability of the biosphere.

Estimated Benefits: Conservation of biodiversity, maintenance of ecosystem services, scientific research, recreation.

2. Arguments in support of the thesis:

Threat to Biodiversity: Human activity (habitat destruction, pollution, climate change) leads to the extinction of species.

Ecosystem Services: Natural ecosystems provide vital services such as water and air purification, plant pollination, climate regulation.

Genetic diversity: A key factor in species’ resilience to disease, climate change and other stressors.

Research: Habitat zones provide opportunities to study natural processes and evolution.

Aesthetic value: Nature has aesthetic value and is important for human well-being.

Economic benefits: Nature-based tourism can generate significant income.

3. Counterarguments (and responses):

Economic costs: Creating and maintaining Zones requires significant investment. Response: Economic benefits (ecosystem services, tourism) may outweigh the costs. In addition, failure to conserve biodiversity may result in much greater economic losses in the long term.

Limitation of land use: Creating Zones may limit economic activity (agriculture, industry). Response: A compromise may be sought that includes sustainable use of resources near the Zones or compensation for the limitation of activity. Strategic planning and zoning are important.

Conflict of interest: Local people may not support the creation of Zones if it affects their way of life.

Answer: It is necessary to involve local populations in the process of planning and managing the Zones, provide them with alternative sources of income and take their interests into account.

Ineffectiveness: The Zones may not be large enough or poorly managed to effectively conserve biodiversity. Answer: Scientific justification for the creation of Zones, monitoring and adaptive management, as well as the creation of ecological corridors connecting the Zones are important.

4. Logical model:

IF (Threat to biodiversity OR Need for ecosystem services OR Importance of genetic diversity) THEN (Creation of Natural Condition Zones of Existence and Species (Genetic) Diversity) THEN (Conservation of species AND Maintenance of ecosystems AND Ensuring genetic stability) 5. Logical proof:

Premise 1: Biodiversity is threatened by human activities.

Premise 2: Conservation of biodiversity is necessary to maintain ecosystem services and the sustainability of the biosphere.

Premise 3: Natural Condition Zones are an effective tool for conserving biodiversity.

Conclusion: Therefore, it is necessary to create Zones of natural conditions of existence and species (genetic) diversity.

6. Logical formula (symbolic):

Let:

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

B = Need for ecosystem services

C = Importance of genetic diversity

D = Zones of natural conditions

E = Conservation of species

F = Maintenance of ecosystems

G = Ensuring genetic stability

Then:

(A OR B OR C) -> D -> (E AND F AND G)

Everything exists and continues for them, thanks to those (who received the Gift from God), whom they destroy. Logical model, logical proof and logical formula.

Analysis and Logical Presentation of the Statement

The statement “Everything exists and continues for them, thanks to those (who received the Gift from God), whom they destroy” can be broken down into the following components:

“They”: Some group/entity (let’s denote it as G)

“Everything exists and continues”: Well-being, existence, progress (let’s denote it as E)

“Gift from God”: Special abilities, talent, knowledge, granted by some higher power (let’s denote it as D)

“Those who received the Gift from God”: A group of people/entities possessing D (let’s denote it as A)

“Exterminate”: Destroy, suppress (let’s denote it as I)

Then the statement can be rephrased: The well-being of G depends on the group A, which G destroys.

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

Logical Model

This can be represented as a cause-and-effect relationship: Cause: A has D.

Effect 1: D promotes E. (A gift from God promotes well-being) Action: G commits I on A. (G exterminates A)

Effect 2: Despite I, E for G continues/improves.

Logical Proof (counterargument)

The presented model itself is not a logical proof, since it rather states a certain situation. Moreover, it looks paradoxical and requires critical analysis. To prove it, the following elements must be confirmed: The existence of a connection between D and E. It must be shown that it is the “gift from God” (D) that is a necessary condition for “well-being” (E). Alternative causes for E must be excluded.

Confirmation of the fact of extermination (I). It is necessary to prove that group G really does deliberately exterminate group A.

The paradox of E continuing after I. The most difficult part is to explain why, despite the destruction of the source of D (group A), G continues to prosper (E). Possible explanations:Inertia: G benefits from A's previous achievements before the moment of extermination.

Exploitation: G uses the fruits of A's labor before the moment of extermination.

Hidden causes: G prospers for other reasons, and the extermination of A is a coincidence or even counterproductive action.

Substitution: G, having exterminated A, replaces them (e.g. by appropriating their knowledge or talents) or finds alternative sources of E.

The main counterargument: The claim assumes that G's well-being depends entirely on those whom G

exterminates. This is unlikely. Usually, well-being depends on many factors.

Logical Formula (attempt at formalization)

Although it is difficult to present this as a strict logical formula, we can make an attempt: Let:

E(G) - Wealth of G

D(A) - A has the Gift

I(G, A) - G destroys A

→ - “entails” (logical consequence)

∧ - “and” (logical AND)

∃ - “exists” (existential quantifier)

Then:

∃ A ∃ G : (D(A) → E(G)) ∧ I(G, A) ∧ E(G)

Which roughly means: “There are groups A and G such that: if A has the Gift, then this entails the welfare of G, and at the same time G destroys A, and G still prospers.”

Important: This formula only tries to reflect the essence of the statement, but is not a strict logical proof or refutation. It only helps to formalize individual elements.

In conclusion: The statement contains a paradox and requires critical analysis. To “prove” it, it is necessary to provide convincing arguments in favor of the direct and exclusive dependence of the well-being of one group on the destruction of another, which has a unique gift. In most cases, such a dependence is neither direct nor exclusive, which makes the original statement controversial.

Everything exists and continues for them, thanks to those (who received the Gift from God), whom they destroy. And if they are destroyed, there will be no existence and its continuation for those who destroy them. Logical model, logical proof and logical formula.

Logical model:

Two groups:

Group A (Who received the Gift from God): Have a certain gift, necessary for the existence and continuation of both groups.

Group B (Destroyers): Destroy group A.

The existence and continuation of group B depends on the existence of group A.

The destruction of group A will lead to the cessation of the existence of group B.

Contradiction:

Group B, by destroying group A, undermines its own existence.

Logical proof:

Initial condition: The existence and continuation of group B depends on the existence of group A. (Given in the statement).

Action of group B: Group B destroys group A. (Given in the statement).

Corollary to 1 and 2: The destruction of group A ends the dependence of group B on group A.

Corollary to 1: The termination of the dependence of group B on group A ends the existence of group B.

(Since dependence was necessary for existence).

Conclusion: Therefore, the action of group B (destruction of group A) leads to the termination of the existence of group B.

CA: Continued existence of group A.

CB: Continued existence of group B.

D(B, A): Dependence of the existence of B on A.

I(B, A): Destruction of group A by B.

Formula:

(D(EB, EA) ∧ D(CB, CA)) ∧ I(B, A) => ¬EB ∧ ¬CB

I(B, A): B destroys A.

=>: Therefore

¬EB ∧ ¬CB: B does not exist And the continued existence of B is impossible.

In conclusion: The statement demonstrates self-destructive behavior. The group, by destroying the source of its existence, dooms itself to destruction. This is a classic logical trap.

Formal systems destroy everything that goes beyond their framework, therefore they destroy life and its continuation, since Life goes beyond all frameworks. Write a logical model, logical proof and logical formula of the statement.

An interesting philosophical thought touching upon the limitations of formal systems and their influence on life. Let's analyze this statement from a logical point of view.

1. Formal system and its limitations

A formal system is a set of rules, axioms, and conclusions that determine how one can work with a certain area of knowledge. It is strictly limited by its axioms and conclusions. However, if we admit that life itself goes beyond any formal systems, then formal systems, not recognizing such deviations, will hinder its continuation and development.

2. Logical model

Suppose that ( L ) is the space of life, and ( F ) is the space of formal systems. We can distinguish: ( L ) is the set of all living beings, processes, and phenomena that can be described only partially by formal systems.

( F ) is the set of all formal systems that have strictly defined rules.

Now, if ( L ) goes beyond ( F ), then the system ( F ) cannot adequately describe or support life ( L ).

Accordingly, any formal system will either limit the development of life or destroy it, since it cannot fully account for all aspects of life.

3. Logical Proof

To present a logical proof, we can consider the following statement: ( F ) limits the description of the phenomena included in ( L ), because it is strictly limited by its axioms and conclusions.

If ( L ) goes beyond ( F ), then no formal system can adequately describe all the phenomena related to life.

Since life is not limited by rules that can be expressed within a formal system ( F ), any system ( F ) that attempts to "govern" or "control" life will be either wrong or destructive of life.

Thus, the statement:

Formal systems, being limited in their understanding of the world, inevitably destroy life and its continuation, since life goes beyond their framework.

4. Logical formula

The following symbols can be used to formalize this statement: ( L ): the space of life.

( F ): the space of formal systems.

( \forall x \in L , (x \notin F) ): for every element ( x ) of life, there exists ( x ) that cannot be described within the formal system.

( \exists f \in F , (f(x) = 0) ): there exists a formal system ( f ) that cannot describe the element ( x ), since it goes beyond its framework.

The logical formula will look like this:

∀x∈L ∃f∈F (f(x)=0)

Where ( f(x) = 0 ) means that the formal system ( f ) cannot correctly interpret ( x ) as an element of life.

This proof can be interpreted as a statement that an attempt to use a limited formal system to explain and control life will lead to its destruction or ineffectiveness.

If everything depends on one thing, it leads to disaster. Logical model, logical proof and logical formula.

Dependence on just one element is a dangerous situation, and here is how it can be expressed using logical tools:

Logical model:

Let's imagine the situation as a system with elements and connections.

Elements: A1, A2, ..., An (where Ai are individual elements of the system) and D (the element on which everything depends).

Connections: Ai -> D (each element Ai depends on D). And D -> (the well-being of the system) (the well-being of the entire system depends on D).

Logical proof (proof by contradiction):

Assumption: The system is stable, even if D fails.

Corollary: If D fails, then Ai also fails (since Ai -> D).

Consequence: If all Ai fail, then the entire system collapses (since the functioning of the system depends on Ai).

Contradiction: We assumed that the system is stable when D fails, but we came to the conclusion that it collapses.

Conclusion: The initial assumption is wrong. The system is NOT stable if D fails. This leads to a disaster.

Logical formula (simplified version):

Let: S = System is OK (System is functioning)

D = Element D is functioning

¬D = Element D is not functioning (failure)

Then: D → S (If D is functioning, then the System is functioning) ¬D → ¬S (If D is not functioning, then the System is not functioning) - This is a potential disaster.

Explanation and Analogies:

Single Point of Failure (SPOF) Vulnerability: In computer science, this is a classic problem. For example, if a single database server fails, the entire application stops working.

Single Resource Economy: A country whose economy is based solely on oil production will suffer greatly if oil prices fall.

The only Guarantee of the continued existence of Russia and its satellite countries can only be the implementation of the Will of God, and this also applies to the rest of the world.

An attempt to logically analyze a statement containing a religious premise inevitably encounters the fact that the premise itself cannot be empirically verified and logically analyzed within the framework of formal logic. Nevertheless, it is possible to identify the components of the statement and consider possible interpretations.

Statement: “The only Guarantee of the continued existence of Russia and its satellite countries can only be the implementation of the Will of God, and this also applies to the rest of the world.”

Components:

Subject: Russia and its satellite countries (and the rest of the world).

Necessary condition for continued existence: Implementation of the Will of God.

Statement of exclusivity: Implementation of the Will of God is the only guarantee.

Problems with logical analysis:

Ambiguity of the “Will of God”: What exactly is meant by the “Will of God”? Without a clear definition, it is impossible to verify whether this will is being realized, or to assess its impact on the existence of Russia and other countries. Different people and religions may have different ideas about the will of God.

Non-falsifiability: The statement is difficult to refute. If Russia (or another country) continues to exist, this can be explained by the realization of the Will of God. If it ceases to exist, this can also be explained by the violation of the Will of God. Such an explanation makes the statement non-falsifiable in the scientific file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

sense.

Exclusivity: The statement excludes any other possible factors influencing the existence of the country (economics, politics, military power, geography, demography, science, technology, international relations, etc.). This statement looks extremely simplified and unrealistic.

Logical model (an attempt at formalization, but with reservations): Let S = Existence of Russia and its satellite countries (and the rest of the World).

Let W = Realization of the Will of the Lord God.

The statement can be represented as: S → W (If S exists, then W must exist).

The exclusiveness of the statement can be represented as: S ↔ W (S exists if and only if W exists).

Logical “proof”:

In a strict sense, logical proof is impossible here due to the indeterminacy of W. One can only present arguments for or against the statement, but these will depend on the interpretation of W.

Argument “for” (religious): The world was created by God and is maintained by His will. Therefore, the existence of everything, including Russia, depends on compliance with His will. (This argument accepts the religious premise as true.)

Argument “against” (rational): The existence of countries is determined by many factors that can be studied and influenced. The statement about the only guarantee ignores these factors and has no empirical support.

Logical formula (with restrictions):

∀x (Exists(Russia(x)) ∧ Exists(Satellite_Countries(x)) ∧ Exists(World(x)) → DependsOn(x, God's_Will)) ∧

¬∃y (y ≠ God's_Will ∧ DependsOn(x, y))

Where:

∀x: For all x (Russia, satellite countries, World)

Exists(x): x exists

DependsOn(x, y): Existence of x depends on y

God's_Will: Will of the Lord God

¬∃y: There is no such y

This formula formalizes the statement that for all entities (Russia, satellite countries, World) their existence depends on the Will of God, and there is no other reason (y) for their existence. This formula clearly shows the exclusivity of the statement.

They show a logical structure, but they cannot resolve the problem of the uncertainty of the key concept (“Will of the Lord God”) and do not take into account many other factors that influence the existence of countries. The diagram reflects the exclusiveness of the statement, where only the implementation of the Will of God determines existence.

The Human Body: If the single organ responsible for filtering toxins (like the liver) stops working, it will result in death.

Why it is a disaster:

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

When all the critical functions and elements of a system depend on a single component, the system has no reserves and no way to cope with the failure of that component. There is no diversity, no redundancy, no bypass paths. Any malfunction, breakdown, or attack on that single component leads to a cascading failure and, ultimately, a disaster.

In conclusion, depending on just one element is a risky strategy that can lead to serious consequences. It is important to strive for diversification, redundancy, and the creation of systems that are resilient to failure.

Most problems are caused by the results of actions.

Let's break this statement down logically:

1. Logical Model:

Premise 1 (P1): Actions lead to consequences.

Premise 2 (P2): Problems are a type of consequence.

Claim (C): Most problems are the result of actions.

2. Logical Proof (Informal):

If we accept that actions always have consequences (P1), and that problems are a type of consequence (P2), then it is logical to conclude that problems arise from actions. The statement then elaborates on this by saying that most problems have this origin. This suggests that there are other sources of problems besides actions, but they are less common.

3. Logical formula (formal, propositional logic):

A: Action

C: Consequence

Pr: Problem

Formula: ∀A (A → C) ∧ ∀Pr (Pr ∈ C) → Most(Pr) ∈ Actions(C)

Where:

∀A (A → C): For all actions A, A entails C (Actions lead to consequences).

∀Pr (Pr ∈ C): For all problems Pr, Pr is an element of C (Problems are one type of consequences).

Most(Pr) ∈ Actions(C): Most of the problems Pr belong to the consequences of actions. (Most of the problems are the result of actions).

4. Mermaid Diagram:

graph LR

A[Action] --> B(Consequence)

C[Problem] --> B

B --> D{Most problems?}

D -- Yes --> E[Result of action]

D -- No --> F[Other causes]

style B fill:#ccf,stroke:#333,stroke-width:2px

style C fill:#f9f,stroke:#333,stroke-width:2px

style D fill:#ccf,stroke:#333,stroke-width:2px

style E fill:#f9f,stroke:#333,stroke-width:2px

style F fill:#ccf,stroke:#333,stroke-width:2px

mermaid

Diagram Explanation:

Action: Represents the actions performed by the subject.

Consequence: Represents the consequences that arise as a result of actions. Problems are one type of consequence.

Problem: Represents the problems that have arisen.

Most Problems?: A conditional statement that tests whether most problems are the result of actions.

Result of Action: If the answer is “Yes”, then the problem is classified as a result of the action.

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

Other Causes: If the answer is “No”, then the problem has another origin.

It is important to note:

The statement refers to “most” problems, not “all” problems. This acknowledges that there are problems caused by external factors, chance, natural phenomena, etc.

The logical formula and the Mermaid diagram simplify a complex reality. Cause and effect relationships can be very complex and multifactorial.

This model focuses on the direct consequences of actions. Indirect and delayed effects can also be significant.

As long as the "Divines" have something, they will continue to "fly in the clouds".

Analysis of the statement “As long as the ‘Divines’ have something to eat, they will continue to ‘fly in the clouds’.”

Logical model:

This statement can be considered as conditional, where:

Premise: “The ‘Divines’ have something to eat.” (Wealth, power, privilege, influence, etc.) Conclusion: “The ‘Divines’ will continue to ‘fly in the clouds’.” (Detachment from reality, unwillingness to see problems, inflated self-esteem, utopian views, etc.)

Logical proof (within the framework of this statement, with the assumption of its truth): The statement assumes a cause-and-effect relationship. The presence of resources and privileges among the “Divines” leads to their detachment from reality. The proof is the assumption that the possession of these resources weakens the need to interact with real problems and motivates the maintenance of a comfortable, but isolated worldview. However, it is important to emphasize that this is not an axiom and can be challenged by counterexamples.

Logical formula:

Let:

P = “The ‘Divines’ have something to eat”

Q = “The ‘Divines’ will ‘fly in the clouds’”

Then the statement can be represented by the formula:

P -> Q (If P, then Q)

This formula means “If the ‘Divines’ have something to eat, then they will ‘fly in the clouds’”. This is a statement of implication. It is important to remember that the truth of P -> Q does not mean that Q is caused by P, but only that if P is true, then Q must also be true. If P is false, then P -> Q is always true (the so-called “ex falso quodlibet”).

Mermaid Diagram:

graph LR

A["The 'Divines' have what they have (P)"] --> B{"If P"}

B --> C["'The Divines' will 'fly in the clouds' (Q)"]

C --> D{"Then Q"}

style C fill:#ccf,stroke:#333,stroke-width:2px

mermaid

Diagram Explanation:

A: Represents the premise (P) - the 'Divines' have resources.

B: Represents the conditional statement 'If P'.

C: Represents the conclusion (Q) - the 'Divines' are detached from reality.

D: Represents the result of the conditional statement 'Then Q'.

The diagram visualizes the logical structure of the statement, showing how the premise leads to the file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

conclusion. The arrows indicate the direction of the causal relationship implied by the statement.

It is important to note:

This analysis and formula are a formalization of the statement, but are not proof of its truth in the real world. The truth of the statement depends on the context and requires empirical verification.

The use of the terms “Divine” and “Flying in the clouds” implies metaphoricality and requires interpretation in each specific case.

The logical formula P -> Q only states the relationship between P and Q, but does not explain the reasons for this relationship. Further research is needed to establish whether P actually causes Q, or whether the relationship between them is accidental or due to other factors.

If there is a reason, there will be.

Based on the principle of whether the West has the ability to suppress Russia's nuclear potential, and with what probability (percentage of probability).

If we consider only the principle itself "whatever has a reason, there will be" and abstract ourselves from the specific geopolitical situation, then the answer will be as follows: If there are sufficient reasons (opportunities, resources, motivation, lack of deterrents) for the West to suppress Russia's nuclear potential, then this will happen.

However, there is a key problem here:

Definition of "sufficient reasons": What exactly should be considered "sufficient reasons"? Elimination of the nuclear threat is certainly a reason. But reasons counteracting this, such as fear of mutual destruction, political costs, moral considerations, are also strong.

The principle of determinism: The principle "whatever has a reason, there will be" implies determinism, that is, predetermination. In complex systems, such as international politics, determinism is questionable.

Events are shaped by the interaction of many factors, many of which are unpredictable.

In other words:

If we accept strict determinism and claim that all events are predetermined, then if the suppression of Russia's nuclear potential by the West occurs, this means that the reasons for this already exist and were predetermined from the very beginning. Conversely, if this does not happen, this means that the reasons for this were absent and were predetermined.

This is a tautology.

The principle itself does not give us any information about the probability or possibility of an event. It only states that events occur when the necessary conditions exist for this.

In conclusion:

If we consider the question only from the point of view of the principle “what has a cause will happen”, the answer will be evasive and uninformative. The principle does not give us tools for analyzing the probability or possibility of an event, especially in complex systems. For a more meaningful answer, it is necessary to take into account specific factors, such as the political situation, military capabilities, economic conditions, etc. Well, if we focus on the “necessary conditions” for the West to suppress Russia’s nuclear potential, we can formulate them like this: For the West to suppress Russia’s nuclear potential, the following conditions are necessary (this is not an exhaustive list, but only the main ones):

Motivation/Determination: The West must have a strong enough motivation to do so that it outweighs the risk of nuclear war and other negative consequences. This means that the West must believe that the threat posed by Russian nuclear weapons is so great that eliminating them is worth the risk of destruction.

Military Capabilities: The West must have military capabilities sufficient to neutralize or destroy a file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

significant portion of the Russian nuclear arsenal before it can be used. This implies superiority in intelligence, precision weapons, cyber warfare, and possibly missile defense.

Russian Weakness: Russia must be in a position where its ability to respond to a Western attack is significantly weakened. This could be due to domestic political problems, an economic crisis, military defeat in another conflict, or a successful disinformation campaign and erosion of confidence in the chain of command.

Lack of Effective Deterrence: Russia must be unable to effectively deter the West from attack. This could be due to the destruction or weakening of Russia's early warning, command and control systems, or a loss of confidence in Russia's nuclear doctrine.

Western Unity: The West must act as a united front, with a common strategy and a willingness to bear risks. This implies the absence of divisions among allies and coordination among key players (US, NATO).

Public Acceptance (of the West): Public opinion in the West must be prepared to support such a move, an operation that increases the risk of catastrophic consequences.

“Necessary” conditions do not mean “sufficient”. Even if all these conditions are present, there is no guarantee that the operation will succeed, or that the consequences will be acceptable.

Assessing the “sufficiency” of each condition is a complex task, requiring an in-depth analysis of intelligence, military capabilities, and the political situation.

In the context of the original principle of “what has a cause, will happen,” it can be said that if and only if all of the above (and other, unnamed) “necessary conditions” are met to a sufficient degree, the West will suppress Russia’s nuclear potential. However, the probability of all of these conditions being met simultaneously remains extremely low.

Reason: The imminent threat of a Russian nuclear strike on the West.

What is the influence of Darwinism on the End of the World?

Everyone minds their own business and does not mind other people's business - order.

Logical model, proof and formula of the principle "Everyone minds their own business and does not mind other people's business - order"

This statement has deep roots in philosophy, sociology and management theory. Building a logical model, proof and formula requires some abstraction and simplification.

1. Logical Model:

Let's imagine a set of subjects (people, departments, machines, etc.) S = {s1, s2, ..., sn}. Each subject has a set of tasks for which they are responsible (area of responsibility). Let's denote the set of tasks of subject si as Ti.

The condition "Everyone minds their own business" can be interpreted as: Each subject si performs all of its tasks from Ti.

Each task in Ti is performed by subject si.

The condition “Does not do other people’s business” can be interpreted as: Subject si does not perform tasks that are not within its area of responsibility Ti. That is, if task t is performed by subject si, then t ∈ Ti.

“Order” is a state of the system characterized by efficiency, predictability, and the absence of conflicts.

This is a somewhat vague concept, so it needs to be specified for a formal proof. Let’s define “order” as: No duplication: Each task is performed by only one subject.

Completion of all necessary tasks: All tasks necessary to achieve the overall goal of the system are performed.

Minimum costs: Tasks are performed in an optimal manner (with minimal expenditure of resources, time, etc.).

2. Logical Proof (sketch):

The proof will be constructed by contradiction. Let’s assume that the principle “Everyone does their own thing” is violated. This means that either someone does not perform their tasks, or they are doing file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

someone else's. Let's consider both cases: Case 1: Someone does not perform their tasks. If si does not perform tasks from Ti, then either these tasks are not performed at all, or someone else tries to perform them (violating "does not do other people's business"). In the first case, the system's goal is not fully achieved, in the second, there is duplication of effort and, possibly, a conflict of interests. Both options reduce efficiency and predictability, which contradicts the definition of "order".

Case 2: Someone is doing other people's business. If si tries to perform task t, which is not included in Ti, then:Either this task is already being performed by the one responsible for it (duplication, conflict).

Or the one responsible for task t has shifted it to si, which violates his own responsibility.

si may not have sufficient qualifications to perform t, which will lead to a poor-quality result or an increase in costs. In any case, this leads to decreased efficiency, increased costs, and possibly conflicts, which contradicts the definition of “order”.

Therefore, violating the principle “Everyone minds his own business” leads to a deterioration in order in the system. Therefore, observing this principle helps maintain order.

3. Logical Formula (simplified):

We introduce the following predicates:

Responsible(s, t): Subject s is responsible for task t.

Executes(s, t): Subject s executes task t.

Order(system): The system is in a state of order.

Then the principle can be expressed as:

∀s ∈ S, ∀t: (Executes(s, t) ↔ Responsible(s, t)) → Order(system) What is read: “For any subject s in the set of subjects S and for any task t: if subject s executes task t if and only if it is responsible for it, then the system is in a state of order.”

Important notes:

Simplification: This model is a simplification of reality. In real systems, flexibility, cooperation, and mutual assistance are often necessary. A strict division of labor can be ineffective.

Defining “order”: It is extremely important to clearly define what is meant by “order” in a particular system. “Order” can mean different things in different systems.

Responsibility: The model assumes a clear definition of responsibilities. In reality, this can be a complex task.

Dynamic: This model is static. In dynamic systems, areas of responsibility may change over time.

Если к любому значению прибавить переменную время, то любое значение увеличивается до

бесконечности.

If you add the variable time to any value, then any value increases to infinity.

For the system to exist continuously, it is necessary to constantly correct the errors that arise in the system and constantly adapt it to changing conditions; the constant progress of the system causes its degeneration. Logical model, logical proof and logical formula.

You have raised a very interesting and important topic concerning the dynamics and evolution of systems.

Let us analyze each of the aspects touched upon and relate them to the concepts of a logical model, proof and formula.

The main idea: Dynamics of systems and “degeneration”

file:///C/Users/Admin/Downloads/Logic%20Syllogistic%20reasoning%20Algorithms%20Fedorchenko%20MV.txt[27.03.2025 2:29:59]

Your formulation emphasizes the contradiction inherent in any system: The need for adaptation: The system must constantly adapt to the changing environment and eliminate internal shortcomings (errors) in order to remain viable. This requires changes and development.

Potential degeneration from progress: It would seem that progress should improve the system. However, too fast, uncontrolled or inadequate progress can lead to its destabilization, complication, loss of flexibility and, ultimately, to “degeneration”. Degeneration here can be understood as a loss of efficiency, stability or even functionality of the system.

Examples:

Biological evolution: Species adapt to their environment, but over-specialization can make them vulnerable to sudden changes.

Software: Constantly updating and adding new features (progress) can lead to code bloat, more bugs, and slower performance (degeneration).

Organizations: As a company grows and evolves, it requires changes in structure and processes. But over-bureaucratization and hierarchy can lead to decreased efficiency and innovation.

Logical Model, Logical Proof, and Logical Formula: Application in the Context of Systems Now let's look at how the concepts of logic can help us understand and manage these dynamics: Logical Model: This is an abstract representation of a system that describes its components, the relationships between them, and the rules for their operation. It can be expressed formally (e.g. using mathematical equations, graphs, or logical formulas) or informally (e.g. diagrams, process descriptions).

Usage: A logical model allows us to analyze a system, identify potential vulnerabilities, predict behavior, and evaluate the effects of changes. It also allows us to formally express requirements for a system and check whether it meets those requirements.

Example: In the context of software, a logical model might be a specification of the architecture of a system, describing its components, their interactions, and data flows.

Logical Proof: A chain of logical reasoning showing that a certain statement (e.g. “the system will be stable under certain conditions”) follows from given axioms and inference rules. Usage: Proofs are used to check the correctness of logical models, to establish properties of a system (e.g. its security, reliability, or performance), and to justify design decisions.

Example: A formal proof of the correctness of an algorithm used in a control system can ensure that the system will operate correctly under specified conditions.

Logical Formula: It is a formal expression built from logical variables, constants, logical operators (AND, OR, NOT, IMPLICATION, etc.) and quantifiers (existence, universality). Application: Logical formulas are used to represent knowledge about a system, to formulate requirements and constraints, to encode decision rules and for automated analysis and verification of the system.

Example: The formula IF (sensor > threshold) AND (time > start_time) THEN (set_alarm) is a simple rule describing the behavior of an alarm system.

In the context of the original idea: Logical approach to system control Logical models, proofs and formulas can be used to:

Model the dynamics of a system: Develop a logical model that takes into account both the processes of adaptation and improvement and the potential risks of “degeneration”.

Formulate requirements for progress: Determine which changes are acceptable and which may lead to undesirable consequences. This can be expressed in the form of logical formulas describing the constraints on change.

Adaptation strategy development: Create an algorithm that automatically adjusts the system, taking into account its current state, external conditions and pre-set constraints (logically expressed).

Assessment of risks and benefits of changes: Using logical proof, one can try to prove that the proposed change will not violate the basic properties of the system (for example, its stability or security).

Conclusion:

The combination of continuous adaptation and conscious management of progress is the key to the sustainability and longevity of any system. Using formal methods such as logical modeling and proof can help us make more informed decisions and avoid potential pitfalls associated with uncontrolled progress.
